Trembling they sit till the darkness flee and the light shone again upon them , but in the place where they be wont to see cattle , and herd , and dwelling , they behold neither house nor beast , nor man nor smoke ; neither be any one remaining in the green place save these four only .
` Whither have they go , and my host also ? '
cried Manawyddan , and they search the hall , and there be no man , and the castle , and there be none , and in the dwelling that be leave be nothing save wild beast .
For a year these four fed on the meat that Manawyddan and Pryderi kill out hunting , and the honey of the bee that suck the mountain heather .
For a time they desire nothing more , but when the next year begin they grow weary .
` We can not spend our life thus , ' say Manawyddan at last , ` let u go into England and learn some trade by which we may live . '
So they leave Wales , and go to Hereford , and there they make saddle , while Manawyddan fashion blue enamel ornament to put on their trapping .
And so greatly do the townsfolk love these saddle , that no others be buy throughout the whole of Hereford , till the saddler band together and resolve to slay Manawyddan and his companion .
When Pryderi heard of it , he be very wroth , and wish to stay and fight .
But the counsel of Manawyddan prevail , and they move by night to another city .
` What craft shall we follow ? '
ask Pryderi .
` We will make shield , ' answered Manawyddan .
` But do we know anything of that craft ? '
answer Pryderi .
` We will try it , ' say Manawyddan , and they begin to make shield , and fashion them after the shape of the shield they have see ; and these likewise they enamel .
And so greatly do they prosper that no man in the town buy a shield except they have make it , till at length the shield-makers band together a the saddler have do , and resolve to slay them .
But of this they have warning , and by night betook themselves to another town .
` Let u take to make shoe , ' say Manawyddan , ` for there be not any among the shoemaker bold enough to fight u . ' '
I know nothing of make shoe , ' answer Pryderi , who in truth despise so peaceful a craft .
` But I know , ' reply Manawyddan , ` and I will teach thee to stitch .
We will buy the leather ready dress , and will make the shoe from it .
Then straightway he seek the town for the best leather , and for a goldsmith to fashion the clasp , and he himself watch till it be do , so that he might learn for himself .
Soon he become know a ` The Maker of Gold Shoes , ' and prosper so greatly , that as long a one could be buy from him not a shoe be purchase from the shoemaker of the town .
And the craftsman be wroth , and band together to slay them .
` Pryderi , ' say Manawyddan , when he have receive news of it , ` we will not remain in England any longer .
Let u set forth to Dyved . '
So they journey until they come to their land at Narberth .
There they gather their dog round them , and hunt for a year a before .
After that a strange thing happen .
One morning Pryderi and Manawyddan rise up to hunt , and loosen their dog , which run before them , till they come to a small bush .
At the bush , the dog shrink away a if frighten , and return to their master , their hair brisling on their back .
` We must see what be in that bush , ' say Pryderi , and what be in it be a boar , with a skin as white a the snow on the mountain .
And he come out , and make a stand a the dog rush on him , drive on by the men .
Long he stand at bay ; then at last he betook himself to flight , and flee to a castle which be newly build , in a place where no building have ever be know .
Into the castle he run , and the dog after him , and long though their master look and listen , they neither saw nor heard aught concern dog or boar . '
I will go into the castle and get tidings of the dog , ' say Pryderi at last .
` Truly , ' answered Manawyddan , ` thou wouldst do unwisely , for whosoever have cast a spell over this land have set this castle here . ' '
I can not give up my dog , ' reply Pryderi , and to the castle he go .
But within be neither man nor beast ; neither boar nor dog , but only a fountain with marble round it , and on the edge a golden bowl , richly wrought , which please Pryderi greatly .
In a moment he forget about his dog , and go up to the bowl and take hold of it , and his hand stick to the bowl , and his foot to the marble slab , and despair take possession of him .
Till the close of day Manawyddan wait for him , and when the sun be fast sinking , he go home , think that he have stray far .
` Where be thy friend and thy dog ? '
say Rhiannon , and he tell her what have befall Pryderi . '
A good friend hast thou lose , ' answer Rhiannon , and she go up to the castle and through the gate , which be open .
There , in the centre of the courtyard , she behold Pryderi standing , and hasten towards him .
` What dost thou here ? '
she ask , lay her hand on the bowl , and a she speak she too stick fast , and be not able to utter a word .
Then thunder be hear and a veil of darkness descend upon them , and the castle vanish and they with it .
When Kieva , the wife of Pryderi , find that neither her husband nor his mother return to her , she be in such sorrow that she care not whether she live or die .
Manawyddan be grieve also in his heart , and say to her : ` It be not fit that we should stay here , for he have lose our dog and can not get food .
Let u go into England -- it be easy for u to live there . '
So they set forth .
` What craft wilt thou follow ? '
ask Kieva a they go along . '
I shall make shoe a once I do , ' reply he ; and he get all the fine leather in the town and cause gild clasp to be make for the shoe , till everyone flock to buy , and all the shoemaker in the town be idle and band together in anger to kill him .
But luckily Manawyddan get word of it , and he and Kieva leave the town one night and proceed to Narberth , take with him a sheaf of wheat , which he sow in three plot of ground .
And while the wheat be grow up , he hunt and fish , and they have food enough and to spare .
Thus the month pass until the harvest ; and one evening Manawyddan visit the furthest of his field of wheat ; and saw that it be ripe .
` To-morrow I will reap this , ' say he ; but on the morrow when he go to reap the wheat he find nothing but the bare straw .
Filled with dismay he hasten to the second field , and there the corn be ripe and golden .
` To-morrow I will reap this , ' he say , but on the morrow the ear have go , and there be nothing but the bare straw .
` Well , there be still one field leave , ' he say , and when he look at it , it be still fair than the other two .
` To-night I will watch here , ' think he , ` for whosoever carry off the other corn will in like manner take this , and I will know who it be . '
So he hide himself and wait .
The hour slide by , and all be still , so still that Manawyddan well-nigh drop asleep .
But at midnight there arise the loud tumult in the world , and peep out he behold a mighty host of mouse , which could neither be number nor measure .
Each mouse climb up a straw till it bend down with it weight , and then it bite off one of the ear , and carry it away , and there be not one of the straw that have not get a mouse to it .
Full of wrath he rush at the mouse , but he could no more come up with them than if they have be gnat , or bird of the air , save one only which linger behind the rest , and this mouse Manawyddan come up with .
Stooping down he seize it by the tail , and put it in his glove , and tie a piece of string across the opening of the glove , so that the mouse could not escape .
When he enter the hall where Kieva be sit , he light a fire , and hang the glove up on a peg .
` What hast thou there ? '
ask she . '
A thief , ' he answer , ` that I catch rob me . '
` What kind of a thief may it be which thou couldst put in thy glove ? '
say Kieva .
` That I will tell thee , ' he reply , and then he show her how his field of corn have be waste , and how he have watch for the mouse .
` And one be less nimble than the rest , and be now in my glove .
To-morrow I will hang it , and I only wish I have them all . '
` It be a marvel , truly , ' say she , ` yet it would be unseemly for a man of thy dignity to hang a reptile such a this .
Do not meddle with it , but let it go . '
` Woe betide me , ' he cry , ` if I would not hang them all if I could catch them , and such a I have I will hang . '
` Verily , ' say she , ` there be no reason I should succour this reptile , except to prevent discredit unto thee . '
` If I know any cause that I should succour it , I would take thy counsel , ' answered Manawyddan , ` but a I know of none , I be mind to destroy it . '
` Do so then , ' say Kieva .
So he go up a hill and set up two fork on the top , and while he be do this he saw a scholar come towards him , whose clothes be tattered .
Now it be seven year since Manawyddan have see man or beast in that place , and the sight amaze him .
` Good day to thee , my lord , ' say the scholar .
` Good greeting to thee , scholar .
Whence dost thou come ? '
` From sing in England ; but wherefore dost thou ask ? '
` Because for seven year no man hath visit this place . ' '
I wander where I will , ' answer the scholar .
` And what work art thou upon ? ' '
I be about to hang a thief that I catch rob me ! '
` What manner of thief be that ? '
inquire the scholar . '
I see a creature in thy hand like upon a mouse , and ill do it become a man of thy rank to touch a reptile like this .
Let it go free . ' '
I will not let it go free , ' cried Manawyddan . '
I catch it rob me , and it shall suffer the doom of a thief . '
` Lord ! '
say the scholar , ` sooner than see a man like thee at such a work , I would give thee a pound which I have receive a alms to let it go free . ' '
I will not let it go free , neither will I sell it . '
` As thou wilt , lord , ' answer the scholar , and he go his way .
Manawyddan be place the cross-beam on the two fork stick , where the mouse be to hang , when a priest rode past .
` Good-day to thee , lord ; and what art thou do ? ' '
I be hang a thief that I catch rob me . '
` What manner of thief , lord ? ' '
A creature in the form of a mouse .
It have be rob me , and it shall suffer the doom of a thief . '
` Lord , ' say the priest , ` sooner than see thee touch this reptile , I would purchase it freedom . ' '
I will neither sell it nor set it free . '
` It be true that a mouse be worth nothing , but rather than see thee defile thyself with touch such a reptile a this , I will give thee three pound for it . ' '
I will not take any price for it .
It shall be hang a it deserve . '
` Willingly , my lord , if it be thy pleasure . '
And the priest go his way .
Then Manawyddan noose the string about the mouse 's neck , and be about to draw it tight when a bishop , with a great following and horse bear huge pack , come by .
` What work art thou upon ? '
ask the bishop , draw rein .
` Hanging a thief that I catch rob me . '
` But be not that a mouse that I see in thine hand ? '
ask the bishop .
` Yes ; that be the thief , ' answered Manawyddan .
` Well , since I have come at the doom of this reptile , I will ransom it of thee for seven pound , rather than see a man of thy rank touch it .
Loose it , and let it go . ' '
I will not let it loose . ' '
I will give thee four and twenty pound to set it free , ' say the bishop . '
I will not set it free for as much again . '
` If thou wilt not set it free for this , I will give thee all the horse thou seest and the seven load of baggage . ' '
I will not set it free . '
` Then tell me at what price thou wilt loose it , and I will give it . '
` The spell must be take off Rhiannon and Pryderi , ' say Manawyddan .
` That shall be do . '
` But not yet will I loose the mouse .
The charm that have be cast over all my land must be take off likewise . '
` This shall be do also . '
` But not yet will I loose the mouse till I know who she be . '
` She be my wife , ' answer the bishop .
` And wherefore come she to me ? '
ask Manawyddan .
` To despoil thee , ' reply the bishop , ` for it be I who cast the charm over thy land , to avenge Gwawl the son of Clud my friend .
And it be I who throw the spell upon Pryderi to avenge Gwawl for the trick that have be play on him in the game of Badger in the Bag .
And not only be I wroth , but my people likewise , and when it be know that thou wast come to dwell in the land , they beseech me much to change them into mouse , that they might eat thy corn .
The first and the second night it be the men of my own house that destroy thy two field , but on the third night my wife and her lady come to me and beg me to change them also into the shape of mouse , that they might take part in avenge Gwawl .
Therefore I change them .
Yet have she not be ill and slow of foot , thou couldst not have overtake her .
Still , since she be catch , I will restore thee Pryderi and Rhiannon , and will take the charm from off thy land .
I have tell thee who she be ; so now set her free . ' '
I will not set her free , ' answered Manawyddan , 'till thou swear that no vengeance shall be take for his , either upon Pryderi , or upon Rhiannon , or on me . ' '
I will grant thee this boon ; and thou hast do wisely to ask it , for on thy head would have light all the trouble .
Set now my wife free . ' '
I will not set her free till Pryderi and Rhiannon be with me . '
` Behold , here they come , ' say the bishop .
Then Manawyddan hold out his hand and greet Pryderi and Rhiannon , and they seat themselves joyfully on the grass .
` Ah , lord , hast thou not receive all thou didst ask ? '
say the bishop .
` Set now my wife free ! '
` That I will gladly , ' answered Manawyddan , unloose the cord from her neck , and a he do so the bishop strike her with his staff , and she turn into a young woman , the fair that ever be see .
` Look around upon thy land , ' say he , ` and thou wilt see it all till and people , a it be long ago . '
And Manawyddan look , and saw corn grow in the field , and cow and sheep grazing on the hill-side , and hut for the people to dwell in .
And he be satisfy in his soul , but one more question he put to the bishop .
` What spell didst thou lay upon Pryderi and Rhiannon ? '
` Pryderi have have the knocker of the gate of my palace hung about him , and Rhiannon have carry the collar of my ass around her neck , ' say the bishop with a smile .
From the ` Mabinogion . '
The Believing Husbands Once upon a time there dwelt in the land of Erin a young man who be seek a wife , and of all the maiden round about none please him as well a the only daughter of a farmer .
The girl be willing and the father be willing , and very soon they be marry and go to live at the farm .
By and bye the season come when they must cut the peat and pile them up to dry , so that they might have fire in the winter .
So on a fine day the girl and her husband , and the father and his wife all go out upon the moor .
They work hard for many hour , and at length grow hungry , so the young woman be send home to bring them food , and also to give the horse their dinner .
When she go into the stable , she suddenly saw the heavy pack-saddle of the speckle mare just over her head , and she jump and say to herself : ` Suppose that pack-saddle be to fall and kill me , how dreadful it would be ! '
and she sit down just under the pack-saddle she be so much afraid of , and begin to cry .
Now the others out on the moor grow hungry and hungry .
` What can have become of her ? '
ask they , and at length the mother declare that she would wait no longer , and must go and see what have happen .
As the bride be nowhere in the kitchen or the dairy , the old woman go into the stable , where she find her daughter weep bitterly .
` What be the matter , my dove ? '
and the girl answer , between her sob : ` When I come in and saw the pack-saddle over my head , I think how dreadful it would be if it fell and kill me , ' and she cry loud than before .
The old woman strike her hand together : ` Ah , to think of it !
if that be to be , what should I do ? '
and she sit down by her daughter , and they both wring their hand and let their tear flow .
` Something strange must have occur , ' exclaim the old farmer on the moor , who by this time be not only hungry , but cross . '
I must go after them . '
And he go and find them in the stable .
` What be the matter ? '
ask he .
` Oh ! '
reply his wife , ` when our daughter come home , do she not see the pack-saddle over her head , and she think how dreadful it would be if it be to fall and kill her . '
` Ah , to think of it ! '
exclaim he , strike his hand together , and he sit down beside them and weep too .
As soon a night fell the young man return full of hunger , and there they be , all cry together in the stable .
` What be the matter ? '
ask he .
` When thy wife come home , ' answer the farmer , ` she saw the pack-saddle over her head , and she think how dreadful it would be if it be to fall and kill her . '
` Well , but it do n't fall , ' reply the young man , and he go off to the kitchen to get some supper , leave them to cry as long a they like .
The next morning he get up with the sun , and say to the old man and to the old woman and to his wife : ` Farewell : my foot shall not return to the house till I have find other three people as silly a you , ' and he walk away till he come to the town , and see the door of a cottage stand open wide , he enter .
No man be present , but only some woman spin at their wheel .
` You do not belong to this town , ' say he .
` You speak truth , ' they answer , ` nor you either ? ' '
I do not , ' reply he , ` but be it a good place to live in ? '
The woman look at each other .
` The men of the town be so silly that we can make them believe anything we please , ' say they .
` Well , here be a gold ring , ' reply he , ` and I will give it to the one amongst you who can make her husband believe the most impossible thing , ' and he leave them .
As soon a the first husband come home his wife say to him : ` Thou art sick ! '
` Am I ? '
ask he .
` Yes , thou art , ' she answer ; ` take off thy clothes and lie down . '
So he do , and when he be in his bed his wife go to him and say : ` Thou art dead . '
` Oh , be I ? '
ask he .
` Thou art , ' say she ; ` shut thine eye and stir neither hand nor foot . '
And dead he felt sure he be .
Soon the second man come home , and his wife say to him : ` You be not my husband ! '
` Oh , be I not ? '
ask he .
` No , it be not you , ' answer she , so he go away and sleep in the wood .
When the third man arrive his wife give him his supper , and after that he go to bed , just a usual .
The next morning a boy knock at the door , bid him attend the burial of the man who be dead , and he be just go to get up when his wife stop him .
` Time enough , ' say she , and he lay still till he hear the funeral pass the window .
` Now rise , and be quick , ' call the wife , and the man jump out of bed in a great hurry , and begin to look about him .
` Why , where be my clothes ? '
ask he .
` Silly that you be , they be on your back , of course , ' answer the woman .
` Are they ? '
say he .
` They be , ' say she , ` and make haste lest the burying be end before you get there . '
Then off he go , run hard , and when the mourner saw a man come towards them with nothing on but his nightshirt , they forget in their fright what they be there for , and flee to hide themselves .
And the naked man stand alone at the head of the coffin .
Very soon a man come out of the wood and speak to him .
` Do you know me ? '
` Not I , ' answer the naked man . '
I do not know you . '
` But why be you naked ? '
ask the first man .
` Am I naked ?
My wife tell me that I have all my clothes on , ' answer he .
` And my wife tell me that I myself be dead , ' say the man in the coffin .
But at the sound of his voice the two men be so terrify that they run straight home , and the man in the coffin get up and follow them , and it be his wife that gain the gold ring , a he have be sillier than the other two .
From ` West Highland Tales . '
The Hoodie-Crow .
Once there live a farmer who have three daughter , and good useful girl they be , up with the sun , and do all the work of the house .
One morning they all run down to the river to wash their clothes , when a hoodie come round and sit on a tree close by .
` Wilt thou wed me , thou farmer 's daughter ? '
he say to the eldest .
` Indeed I wo n't wed thee , ' she answer , ` an ugly brute be the hoodie . '
And the bird , much offend , spread his wing and fly away .
But the following day he come back again , and say to the second girl : ` Wilt thou wed me , farmer 's daughter ? '
` Indeed I will not , ' answer she , ` an ugly brute be the hoodie . '
And the hoodie be more angry than before , and go away in a rage .
However , after a night 's rest he be in a good temper , and think that he might be more lucky the third time , so back he go to the old place .
` Wilt thou wed me , farmer 's daughter ? '
he say to the young .
` Indeed I will wed thee ; a pretty creature be the hoodie , ' answer she , and on the morrow they be marry . '
I have something to ask thee , ' say the hoodie when they be far away in his own house .
` Wouldst thou rather I should be a hoodie by day and a man by night , or a man by day and a hoodie by night ? '
The girl be surprise at his word , for she do not know that he could be anything but a hoodie at all time .
Still she say nothing of this , and only reply , ' I would rather thou wert a man by day and a hoodie by night , ' And so he be ; and a handsomer man or a more beautiful hoodie never be see .
The girl love them both , and never wish for thing to be different .
By and bye they have a son , and very pleased they both be .
But in the night soft music be hear steal close towards the house , and every man slept , and the mother slept also .
When they wake again it be morning , and the baby be go .
High and low they look for it , but nowhere could they find it , and the farmer , who have come to see his daughter , be greatly grieve , a he fear it might be think that he have steal it , because he do not want the hoodie for a son-in-law .
The next year the hoodie 's wife have another son , and this time a watch be set at every door .
But it be no use .
In vain they determine that , come what might , they would not close their eye ; at the first note of music they all fell asleep , and when the farmer arrive in the morning to see his grandson , he find them all weeping , for while they have sleep the baby have vanish .
Well , the next year it all happen again , and the hoodie 's wife be so unhappy that her husband resolve to take her away to another house he have , and her sister with her for company .
So they set out in a coach which be big enough to hold them , and have not go very far when the hoodie suddenly say : ` You be sure you have not forget anything ? ' '
I have forget my coarse comb , ' answer the wife , feel in her pocket , and a she speak the coach change into a withered faggot , and the man become a hoodie again , and fly away .
The two sister return home , but the wife follow the hoodie .
Sometimes she would see him on a hill-top , and then would hasten after him , hop to catch him .
But by the time she have get to the top of the hill , he would be in the valley on the other side .
When night come , and she be tire , she look about for some place to rest , and glad she be to see a little house full of light straight in front of her , and she hurry towards it as fast a she could .
At the door stand a little boy , and the sight of him fill her heart with pleasure , she do not know why .
A woman come out , and bid her welcome , and set before her food , and give her a soft bed to lie on .
And the hoodie 's wife lay down , and so tired be she , that it seem to her but a moment before the sun rise , and she awake again .
From hill to hill she go after the hoodie , and sometimes she saw him on the top ; but when she get to the top , he have fly into the valley , and when she reach the valley he be on the top of another hill -- and so it happen till night come round again .
Then she look round for some place to rest in , and she behold a little house of light before her , and fast she hurry towards it .
At the door stand a little boy , and her heart be fill with pleasure at the sight of him , she do not know why .
After that a woman bid her enter , and set food before her , and give her a soft bed to lie in .
And when the sun rise she get up , and leave the house , in search of the hoodie .
This day everything befell a on the two other day , but when she reach the small house , the woman bid her keep awake , and if the hoodie fly into the room , to try to seize him .
But the wife have walk far , and be very tired , and strive a she would , she fell sound asleep .
Many hour she sleep , and the hoodie enter through a window , and let fall a ring on her hand .
The girl awoke with a start , and leant forward to grasp him , but he be already fly off , and she only seize a feather from his wing .
And when dawn come , she get up and tell the woman .
` He have go over the hill of poison , ' say she , ` and there you can not follow him without horse-shoes on your hand and foot .
But I will help you .
Put on this suit of men 's clothes , and go down this road till you come to the smithy , and there you can learn to make horse-shoes for yourself . '
The girl thank her , and put on the cloth and go down the road to do her bidding .
So hard do she work , that in a few day she be able to make the horse-shoes .
Early one morning she set out for the hill of poison .
On her hand and foot she go , but even with the horse-shoes on she have to be very careful not to stumble , l some poisoned thorn should enter into her flesh , and she should die .
But when at last she be over , it be only to hear that her husband be to be marry that day to the daughter of a great lord .
Now there be to be a race in the town , and everyone meant to be there , except the stranger who have come over the hill of poison -- everyone , that be , but the cook , who be to make the bridal supper .
Greatly he love race , and sore be his heart to think that one should be run without his see it , so when he behold a woman whom he do not know come along the street , hope spring up in him .
` Will you cook the wedding feast in place of me ? '
he say , ` and I will pay you well when I return from the race . '
Gladly she agree , and cook the feast in a kitchen that look into the great hall , where the company be to eat it .
After that she watch the seat where the bridegroom be sit , and take a plateful of the broth , she drop the ring and the feather into it , and set if herself before him .
With the first spoonful he take up the ring , and a thrill run through him ; in the second he behold the feather and rise from his chair .
` Who have cook this feast ? '
ask he , and the real cook , who have come back from the race , be bring before him .
` He may be the cook , but he do not cook this feast , ' say the bridegroom , and then inquiry be make , and the girl be summon to the great hall .
` That be my married wife , ' he declare , ` and no one else will I have , ' and at that very moment the spell fell off him , and never more would he be a hoodie .
Happy indeed be they to be together again , and little do they mind that the hill of poison take long to cross , for she have to go some way forward , and then throw the horse-shoes back for him to put on .
Still , at last they be over , and they go back the way she have come , and stop at the three house in order to take their little son to their own home .
But the story never say who have steal them , nor what the coarse comb have to do with it .
From ` West Highland Tales . '
The Brownie of the Lake Once upon a time there live in France a man whose name be Jalm Riou .
You might have walk a whole day without meet anyone happier or more contented , for he have a large farm , plenty of money , and above all , a daughter call Barbaik , the most graceful dancer and the best-dressed girl in the whole country side .
When she appear on holiday in her embroidered cap , five petticoat , each one a little short than the other , and shoe with silver buckle , the woman be all fill with envy , but little cared Barbaik what they might whisper behind her back as long a she know that her clothes be fine than anyone else 's and that she have more partner than any other girl .
Now amongst all the young men who want to marry Barbaik , the one whose heart be most set on her be her father 's head man , but a his manner be rough and he be exceedingly ugly she would have nothing to say to him , and , what be bad , often make fun of him with the rest .
Jegu , for that be his name , of course heard of this , and it make him very unhappy .
Still he would not leave the farm , and look for work elsewhere , a he might have do , for then he would never see Barbaik at all , and what be life worth to him without that ?
One evening he be bring back his horse from the field , and stop at a little lake on the way home to let them drink .
He be tire with a long day 's work , and stand with his hand on the mane of one of the animal , wait till they have do , and think all the while of Barbaik , when a voice come out of the gorse close by .
` What be the matter , Jegu ?
You must n't despair yet . '
The young man glance up in surprise , and ask who be there .
` It be I , the brownie of the lake , ' reply the voice .
` But where be you ? '
inquired Jegu .
` Look close , and you will see me among the reed in the form of a little green frog .
I can take , ' he add proudly , ` any shape I choose , and even , which be much harder , be invisible if I want to . '
` Then show yourself to me in the shape in which your family generally appear , ' reply Jegu .
` Certainly , if you wish , ' and the frog jump on the back of one of the horse , and change into a little dwarf , all dress in green .
This transformation rather frighten Jegu , but the brownie bid him have no fear , for he would not do him any harm ; indeed , he hop that Jegu might find him of some use .
` But why should you take all this interest in me ? '
ask the peasant suspiciously .
` Because of a service you do me last winter , which I have never forget , ' answer the little fellow .
` You know , I be sure , that the korigans -LSB- FN # 3 : The spiteful fairy . -RSB-
who dwell in the White Corn country have declare war on my people , because they say that they be the friend of man .
We be therefore oblige to take refuge in distant land , and to hide ourselves at first under different animal shape .
Since that time , partly from habit and partly to amuse ourselves , we have continue to transform ourselves , and it be in this way that I get to know you . '
` How ? '
exclaim Jegu , fill with astonishment .
` Do you remember when you be dig in the field near the river , three month ago , you find a robin redbreast caught in a net ?
` Yes , ' answer Jegu , ' I remember it very well , and I open the net and let him go . '
` Well , I be that robin redbreast , and ever since I have vow to be your friend , and a you want to marry Barbaik , I will prove the truth of what I say by help you to do so . '
` Ah !
my little brownie , if you can do that , there be nothing I wo n't give you , except my soul . '
` Then let me alone , ' rejoin the dwarf , ` and I promise you that in a very few month you shall be master of the farm and of Barbaik . '
` But how be you go to do it ? '
exclaim Jegu wonderingly .
` That be my affair .
Perhaps I may tell you later .
Meanwhile you just eat and sleep , and do n't worry yourself about anything . '
Jegu declare that nothing could be easy , and then take off his hat , he thank the dwarf heartily , and lead his horse back to the farm .
Next morning be a holiday , and Barbaik be awake early than usual , a she wish to get through her work as soon a possible , and be ready to start for a dance which be to be hold some distance off .
She go first to the cow-house , which it be her duty to keep clean , but to her amazement she find fresh straw put down , the rack fill with hay , the cow milk , and the pail stand neatly in a row .
` Of course , Jegu must have do this in the hope of my give him a dance , ' she think to herself , and when she meet him outside the door she stop and thank him for his help .
To be sure , Jegu only reply roughly that he do n't know what she be talk about , but this answer make her feel all the more certain that it be he and nobody else .
The same thing take place every day , and never have the cow-house be so clean nor the cow so fat .
Morning and even Barbaik find her earthen pots full of milk and a pound of butter freshly churn , ornament with leaf .
At the end of a few week she grow so used to this state of affair that she only get up just in time to prepare breakfast .
Soon even this grow to be unnecessary , for a day arrive when , come downstairs , she discover that the house be sweep , the furniture polish , the fire lit , and the food ready , so that she have nothing to do except to ring the great bell which summon the labourer from the field to come and eat it .
This , also , she think be the work of Jegu , and she could not help feeling that a husband of this sort would be very useful to a girl who like to lie in bed and to amuse herself .
Indeed , Barbaik have only to express a wish for it to be satisfy .
If the wind be cold or the sun be hot and she be afraid to go out l her complexion should be spoil , she need only to run down to the spring close by and say softly , ' I should like my churn to be full , and my wet linen to be stretch on the hedge to dry , ' and she need never give another thought to the matter .
If she find the rye bread too hard to bake , or the oven take too long to heat , she just murmur , ' I should like to see my six loaf on the shelf above the bread box , ' and two hour after there they be .
If she be too lazy to walk all the way to market along a dirty road , she would say out loud the night before , ` Why be I not already back from Morlaix with my milk pot empty , my butter bowl inside it , a pound of wild cherry on my wooden plate , and the money I have gain in my apron pocket ? '
and in the morning when she get up , lo and behold !
there be stand at the foot of her bed the empty milk pot with the butter bowl inside , the black cherry on the wooden plate , and six new piece of silver in the pocket of her apron .
And she believe that all this be owe to Jegu , and she could no longer do without him , even in her thought .
When thing have reach this pas , the brownie tell the young man that he have well ask Barbaik to marry him , and this time the girl do not turn rudely away , but listen patiently to the end .
In her eye he be a ugly and awkward a ever , but he would certainly make a most useful husband , and she could sleep every morning till breakfast time , just like a young lady , and a for the rest of the day , it would not be half long enough for all she mean to do .
She would wear the beautiful dress that come when she wish for them , and visit her neighbour , who would be die of envy all the while , and she would be able to dance as much a she wish .
Jegu would always be there to work for her and save for her , and watch over her .
So , like a well-brought-up girl , Barbaik answer that it should be a her father pleased , know quite well that old Riou have often say that after he be dead there be no one so capable of carry on the farm .
The marriage take place the follow month , and a few day later the old man die quite suddenly .
Now Jegu have everything to see to himself , and somehow it do not seem so easy a when the farmer be alive .
But once more the brownie step in , and be good than ten labourer .
It be he who plough and sow and reap , and if , a happen , occasionally , it be needful to get the work do quickly , the brownie call in some of his friend , and as soon a it be light a host of little dwarf might have be see in the field , busy with hoe , fork or sickle .
But by the time the people be about all be finish , and the little fellow have disappear .
And all the payment the brownie ever ask for be a bowl of broth .
From the very day of her marriage Barbaik have note with surprise and rage that thing cease to be do for her a they have be do all the week and month before .
She complain to Jegu of his laziness , and he only star at her , not understand what she be talk about .
But the brownie , who be stand by , burst out laughing , and confess that all the good office she speak of have be perform by him , for the sake of Jegu , but that now he have other business to do , and it be high time that she look after her house herself .
Barbaik be furious .
Each morning when she be oblige to get up before dawn to milk the cow and go to market , and each evening when she have to sit up till midnight in order to churn the butter , her heart be fill with rage against the brownie who have cause her to expect a life of ease and pleasure .
But when she look at Jegu and behold his red face , squint eye , and untidy hair , her anger be double .
` If it have not be for you , you miserable dwarf ! '
she would say between her teeth , ` if it have not be for you I should never have marry that man , and I should still have be go to dance , where the young men would have bring me present of nut and cherry , and tell me that I be the pretty girl in the parish .
While now I can receive no present except from my husband .
I can never dance , except with my husband .
Oh , you wretched dwarf , I will never , never forgive you ! '
In spite of her fierce word , no one know good than Barbaik how to put her pride in her pocket when it suit her , and after receive an invitation to a wedding , she beg the brownie to get her a horse to ride there .
To her great joy he consent , bid her set out for the city of the dwarf and to tell them exactly what she want .
Full of excitement , Barbaik start on her journey .
It be not long , and when she reach the town she go straight to the dwarf , who be hold counsel in a wide green place , and say to them , ` Listen , my friend !
I have come to beg you to lend me a black horse , with eye , a mouth , ear , bridle and saddle . '
She have hardly speak when the horse appear , and mount on his back she start for the village where the wedding be to be hold .
At first she be so delighted with the chance of a holiday from the work which she hat , that she notice nothing , but very soon it strike her a odd that a she pass along the road full of people they all laugh a they look at her horse .
At length she catch some word utter by one man to another .
` Why , the farmer 's wife have sell her horse 's tail ! '
and turn in her saddle .
Yes ; it be true .
Her horse have no tail !
She have forget to ask for one , and the wicked dwarf have carry out her order to the letter !
` Well , at any rate , I shall soon be there , ' she think , and shake the rein , try to urge the horse to a gallop .
But it be of no use ; he decline to move out of a walk ; and she be force to hear all the joke that be make upon her .
In the evening she return to the farm more angry than ever , and quite determine to revenge herself on the brownie whenever she have the chance , which happen to be very soon .
It be the spring , and just the time of year when the dwarf hold their fete , so one day the brownie ask Jegu if he might bring his friend to have supper in the great barn , and whether he would allow them to dance there .
Of course , Jegu be only too pleased to be able to do anything for the brownie , and he order Barbaik to spread her best table-cloths in the barn , and to make a quantity of little loaf and pancake , and , besides , to keep all the milk give by the cow that morning .
He expect she would refuse , a he know she hat the dwarf , but she say nothing , and prepare the supper a he have bid her .
When all be ready , the dwarf , in new green suit , come bustling in , very happy and merry , and take their seat at the table .
But in a moment they all spring up with a cry , and run away scream , for Barbaik have place pan of hot coal under their foot , and all their poor little toe be burnt .
` You wo n't forget that in a hurry , ' she say , smile grimly to herself , but in a moment they be back again with large pot of water , which they pour on the fire .
Then they join hand and dance round it , sing : Wicked traitress , Barne Riou , Our poor toe be burn by you ; Now we hurry from your hall -- Bad luck light upon you all .
That even they leave the country for ever , and Jegu , without their help , grow poor and poor , and at last died of misery , while Barbaik be glad to find work in the market of Morlaix .
From ` Le Foyer Breton , ' par E. Souvestre .
The Winning of Olwen There be once a king and queen who have a little boy , and they call his name Kilweh .
The queen , his mother , fell ill soon after his birth , and a she could not take care of him herself she send him to a woman she know up in the mountain , so that he might learn to go out in all weather , and bear heat and cold , and grow tall and strong .
Kilweh be quite happy with his nurse , and run race and climb hill with the child who be his playfellow , and in the winter , when the snow lay on the ground , sometimes a man with a harp would stop and beg for shelter , and in return would sing them song of strange thing that have happen in the year go by .
But long before this change have take place in the court of Kilweh 's father .
Soon after she have send her baby away the queen become much bad , and at length , see that she be go to die , she call her husband to her and say : ` Never again shall I rise from this bed , and by and bye thou wilt take another wife .
But l she should make thee forget thy son , I charge thee that thou take not a wife until thou see a briar with two blossom upon my grave . '
And this he promise her .
Then she far bid him to see to her grave that nothing might grow thereon .
This likewise he promise her , and soon she die , and for seven year the king send a man every morning to see that nothing be grow on the queen 's grave , but at the end of seven year he forget .
One day when the king be out hunt he ride past the place where the queen lay bury , and there he saw a briar grow with two blossom on it .
` It be time that I take a wife , ' say he , and after long look he find one .
But he do not tell her about his son ; indeed he hardly remember that he have one till she hear it at last from an old woman whom she have go to visit .
And the new queen be very pleased , and send messenger to fetch the boy , and in his father 's court he stay , while the year go by till one day the queen tell him that a prophecy have foretell that he be to win for his wife Olwen the daughter of Yspaddaden Penkawr .
When he hear this Kilweh felt proud and happy .
Surely he must be a man now , he think , or there would be no talk of a wife for him , and his mind dwell all day upon his promised bride , and what she would be like when he behold her .
` What aileth thee , my son ? '
ask his father at last , when Kilweh have forget something he have be bid to do , and Kilweh blush red a he answer : ` My stepmother say that none but Olwen , the daughter of Yspaddaden Penkawr , shall be my wife . '
` That will be easily fulfil , ' reply his father .
` Arthur the king be thy cousin .
Go therefore unto him and beg him to cut thy hair , and to grant thee this boon . '
Then the youth prick forth upon a dapple grey horse of four year old , with a bridle of link gold , and gold upon his saddle .
In his hand he bore two spear of silver with head of steel ; a war-horn of ivory be sling round his shoulder , and by his side hang a golden sword .
Before him be two brindled white-breasted greyhound with collar of ruby round their neck , and the one that be on the left side bound across to the right side , and the one on the right to the left , and like two sea-swallows sport round him .
And his horse cast up four sod with his four hoof , like four swallow in the air about his head , now above , now below .
About him be a robe of purple , and an apple of gold be at each corner , and every one of the apple be of the value of a hundred cow .
And the blade of grass bent not beneath him , so light be his horse 's foot a he journey toward the gate of Arthur 's palace .
` Is there a porter ? '
cry Kilweh , look round for someone to open the gate .
` There be ; and I be Arthur 's porter every first day of January , ' answer a man come out to him .
` The rest of the year there be other porter , and among them Pennpingyon , who go upon his head to save his foot . '
` Well , open the portal , I say . '
` No , that I may not do , for none can enter save the son of a king or a pedlar who have good to sell .
But elsewhere there will be food for thy dog and hay for thy horse , and for thee collops cook and pepper , and sweet wine shall be serve in the guest chamber . '
` That will not do for me , ' answer Kilweh .
` If thou wilt not open the gate I will send up three shout that shall be hear from Cornwall unto the north , and yet again to Ireland . '
` Whatsoever clamour thou mayest make , ' spake Glewlwyd the porter , ` thou shalt not enter until I first go and speak with Arthur . '
Then Glewlwyd go into the hall , and Arthur say to him : ` Hast thou news from the gate ? '
and the porter answer : ` Far have I travel , both in this island and elsewhere , and many kingly men have I see ; but never yet have I behold one equal in majesty to him who now stand at the door . '
` If walking thou didst enter here , return thou running , ' reply Arthur , ` and let everyone that open and shut the eye show him respect and serve him , for it be not meet to keep such a man in the wind and rain . '
So Glewlwyd unbar the gate and Kilweh rode in upon his charger .
` Greeting unto thee , O ruler of this land , ' cry he , ` and greet no less to the low than to the high . '
` Greeting to thee also , ' answer Arthur .
` Sit thou between two of my warrior , and thou shalt have minstrel before thee and all that belong to one born to be a king , while thou remainest in my palace . ' '
I be not come , ' reply Kilweh , ` for meat and drink , but to obtain a boon , and if thou grant it me I will pay it back , and will carry thy praise to the four wind of heaven .
But if thou wilt not grant it to me , then I will proclaim thy discourtesy wherever thy name be know . '
` What thou askest that shalt thou receive , ' say Arthur , ` as far a the wind dry and the rain moistens , and the sun revolves and the sea encircles and the earth extend .
Save only my ship and my mantle , my word and my lance , my shield and my dagger , and Guinevere my wife . ' '
I would that thou bless my hair , ' spake Kilweh , and Arthur answer : ` That shall be grant thee . '
Forthwith he bid his men fetch him a comb of gold and a scissors with loop of silver , and he comb the hair of Kilweh his guest .
` Tell me who thou art , ' he say , ` for my heart warm to thee , and I feel thou art come of my blood . ' '
I be Kilweh , son of Kilydd , ' reply the youth .
` Then my cousin thou art in truth , ' reply Arthur , ` and whatsoever boon thou mayest ask thou shalt receive . '
` The boon I crave be that thou mayest win for me Olwen , the daughter of Yspaddaden Penkawr , and this boon I seek likewise at the hand of thy warrior .
From Sol , who can stand all day upon one foot ; from Ossol , who , if he be to find himself on the top of the high mountain in the world , could make it into a level plain in the beat of a bird 's wing ; from Cluse , who , though he be bury under the earth , could yet hear the ant leave her nest fifty mile away : from these and from Kai and from Bedwyr and from all thy mighty men I crave this boon . ' '
O Kilweh , ' say Arthur , ` never have I heard of the maiden of whom thou speakest , nor of her kindred , but I will send messenger to seek her if thou wilt give me time . '
` From this night to the end of the year right willingly will I grant thee , ' reply Kilweh ; but when the end of the year come and the messenger return Kilweh be wroth , and speak rough word to Arthur .
It be Kai , the bold of the warrior and the swiftest of foot -- he would could pass nine night without sleep , and nine day beneath the water -- that answer him : ` Rash youth that thou art , darest thou speak thus to Arthur ?
Come with u , and we will not part company till we have win that maiden , or till thou confess that there be none such in the world . '
Then Arthur summon his five best men and bid them go with Kilweh .
There be Bedwyr the one-handed , Kai 's comrade and brother in arm , the swift man in Britain save Arthur ; there be Kynddelig , who know the path in a land where he have never be as surely a he do those of his own country ; there be Gwrhyr , that could speak all tongue ; and Gwalchmai the son of Gwyar , who never return till he have gain what he seek ; and last of all there be Menw , who could weave a spell over them so that none might see them , while they could see everyone .
So these seven journey together till they reach a vast open plain in which be a fair castle .
But though it seem so close it be not until the evening of the third day that they really draw near to it , and in front of it a flock of sheep be spread , so many in number that there seem no end to them .
A shepherd stood on a mound watching over them , and by his side be a dog , as large a a horse nine winter old .
` Whose be this castle , O herdsman ? '
ask the knight .
` Stupid be ye truly , ' answer the herdsman .
` All the world know that this be the castle of Yspaddaden Penkawr . '
` And who art thou ? ' '
I be call Custennin , brother of Yspaddaden , and ill have he treat me .
And who be you , and what do you here ? '
` We come from Arthur the king , to seek Olwen the daughter of Yspaddaden , ' but at this news the shepherd give a cry : ' O men , be warn and turn back while there be yet time .
Others have go on that quest , but none have escape to tell the tale , ' and he rise to his foot a if to leave them .
Then Kilweh hold out to him a ring of gold , and he try to put it on his finger , but it be too small , so he place it in his glove , and go home and give it to his wife .
` Whence come this ring ? '
ask she , ` for such good luck be not wont to befall thee . '
` The man to whom this ring belong thou shalt see here in the evening , ' answer the shepherd ; ` he be Kilweh , son of Kilydd , cousin to king Arthur , and he have come to seek Olwen . '
And when the wife heard that she know that Kilweh be her nephew , and her heart yearn after him , half with joy at the thought of see him , and half with sorrow for the doom she fear .
Soon they hear step approach , and Kai and the rest enter into the house and ate and drank .
After that the woman open a chest , and out of it come a youth with curl yellow hair .
` It be a pity to hide him thus , ' say Gwrhyr , ` for well I know that he have do no evil . '
` Three and twenty of my son have Yspaddaden slain , and I have no more hope of save this one , ' reply she , and Kai be full of sorrow and answer : ` Let him come with me and be my comrade , and he shall never be slain unless I be slain also . '
And so it be agree .
` What be your errand here ? '
ask the woman .
` We seek Olwen the maiden for this youth , ' answered Kai ; ` do she ever come hither so that she may be see ? '
` She come every Saturday to wash her hair , and in the vessel where she wash she leave all her ring , and never do she so much a send a messenger to fetch them . '
` Will she come if she be bidden ? '
ask Kai , pondering .
` She will come ; but unless you pledge me your faith that you will not harm her I will not fetch her . '
` We pledge it , ' say they , and the maiden come .
A fair sight be she in a robe of flame-coloured silk , with a collar of ruddy gold about her neck , bright with emerald and ruby .
More yellow be her head than the flower of the broom , and her skin be white than the foam of the wave , and fairer be her hand than the blossom of the wood anemone .
Four white trefoil spring up where she tread , and therefore be she call Olwen .
She enter , and sit down on a bench beside Kilweh , and he spake to her : ` Ah , maiden , since first I hear thy name I have love thee -- wilt thou not come away with me from this evil place ? '
` That I can not do , ' answer she , ` for I have give my word to my father not to go without his knowledge , for his life will only last till I be betroth .
Whatever be , must be , but this counsel I will give you .
Go , and ask me of my father , and whatsoever he shall require of thee grant it , and thou shalt win me ; but if thou deny him anything thou wilt not obtain me , and it will be well for thee if thou escape with thy life . '
` All this I promise , ' say he .
So she return to the castle , and all Arthur 's men go after her , and enter the hall .
` Greeting to thee , Yspaddaden Penkawr , ' say they .
` We come to ask thy daughter Olwen for Kilweh , son of Kilydd . '
` Come hither to-morrow and I will answer you , ' reply Yspaddaden Penkawr , and a they rise to leave the hall he catch up one of the three poison dart that lay beside him and fling it in their midst .
But Bedwyr saw and catch it , and fling it back so hard that it pierce the knee of Yspaddaden . '
A gentle son-in-law , truly ! '
he cry , writhe with pain . '
I shall ever walk the bad for this rudeness .
Cursed be the smith who forge it , and the anvil on which it be wrought ! '
That night the men sleep in the house of Custennin the herdsman , and the next day they proceed to the castle , and enter the hall , and say : ` Yspaddaden Penkawr , give u thy daughter and thou shalt keep her dower .
And unless thou wilt do this we will slay thee . '
` Her four great grandmother and her four great grandfather yet live , ' answer Yspaddaden Penkawr ; ` it be needful that I take counsel with them . '
` Be it so ; we will go to meat , ' but a they turn he take up the second dart that lay by his side and cast it after them .
And Menw catch it , and fling it at him , and wound him in the chest , so that it come out at his back . '
A gentle son-in-law , truly ! '
cry Yspaddaden , ` the iron pain me like the bite of a horse-leech .
Cursed be the hearth whereon it be heat , and the smith who form it ! '
The third day Arthur 's men return to the palace into the presence of Yspaddaden .
` Shoot not at me again , ' say he , ` unless you desire death .
But lift up my eyebrow , which have fall over my eye , that I may see my son-in-law . '
Then they arise , and a they do so Yspaddaden Penkawr take the third poisoned dart and cast it at them .
And Kilweh catch it , and fling it back , and it pass through his eyeball , and come out on the other side of his head . '
A gentle son-in-law , truly !
Cursed be the fire in which it be forge and the man who fashion it ! '
The next day Arthur 's men come again to the palace and say : ` Shoot not at u any more unless thou desirest more pain than even now thou hast , but give u thy daughter without more word . '
` Where be he that seek my daughter ?
Let him come hither so that I may see him . '
And Kilweh sit himself in a chair and speak face to face with him .
` Is it thou that seekest my daughter ? '
` It be I , ' answer Kilweh .
` First give me thy word that thou wilt do nothing towards me that be not just , and when thou hast win for me that which I shall ask , then thou shalt wed my daughter . ' '
I promise right willingly , ' say Kilweh .
` Name what thou wilt . '
` Seest thou yonder hill ?
Well , in one day it shall be root up and plough and sow , and the grain shall ripen , and of that wheat I will bake the cake for my daughter 's wedding . '
` It will be easy for me to compass this , although thou mayest deem it will not be easy , ' answer Kilweh , thinking of Ossol , under whose foot the high mountain become straightway a plain , but Yspaddaden pay no heed , and continue : ` Seest thou that field yonder ?
When my daughter be bear nine bushel of flax be sown therein , and not one blade have spring up .
I require thee to sow fresh flax in the ground that my daughter may wear a veil spun from it on the day of her wedding . '
` It will be easy for me to compass this . '
` Though thou compass this there be that which thou wilt not compass .
For thou must bring me the basket of Gwyddneu Garanhir which will give meat to the whole world .
It be for thy wedding feast .
Thou must also fetch me the drinking-horn that be never empty , and the harp that never cease to play until it be bidden .
Also the comb and scissors and razor that lie between the two ear of Trwyth the boar , so that I may arrange my hair for the wedding .
And though thou get this yet there be that which thou wilt not get , for Trwyth the boar will not let any man take from him the comb and the scissors , unless Drudwyn the whelp hunt him .
But no leash in the world can hold Drudwyn save the leash of Cant Ewin , and no collar will hold the leash except the collar of Canhastyr . '
` It will be easy for me to compass this , though thou mayest think it will not be easy , ' Kilweh answer him .
` Though thou get all these thing yet there be that which thou wilt not get .
Throughout the world there be none that can hunt with this dog save Mabon the son of Modron .
He be take from his mother when three night old , and it be not know where he now be , nor whether he be live or dead , and though thou find him yet the boar will never be slay save only with the sword of Gwrnach the giant , and if thou obtain it not neither shalt thou obtain my daughter . '
` Horses shall I have , and knight from my lord Arthur .
And I shall gain thy daughter , and thou shalt lose thy life . '
The speech of Kilweh the son of Kilydd with Yspaddaden Penkawr be end .
Then Arthur 's men set forth , and Kilweh with them , and journeyed till they reach the large castle in the world , and a black man come out to meet them .
` Whence comest thou , O man ? '
ask they , ` and whose be that castle ? '
` That be the castle of Gwrnach the giant , a all the world know , ' answer the man , ` but no guest ever return thence alive , and none may enter the gate except a craftsman , who bring his trade . '
But little do Arthur 's men heed his warning , and they go straight to the gate .
` Open ! '
cry Gwrhyr . '
I will not open , ' reply the porter .
` And wherefore ? '
ask Kai .
` The knife be in the meat , and the drink be in the horn , and there be revelry in the hall of Gwrnach the giant , and save for a craftsman who bring his trade the gate will not be open to-night . '
` Verily , then , I may enter , ' say Kai , ` for there be no good burnisher of sword than I. ' ` This will I tell Gwrnach the giant , and I will bring thee his answer . '
` Bid the man come before me , ' cried Gwrnach , when the porter have tell his tale , ` for my sword stand much in need of polish , ' so Kai pass in and salute Gwrnach the giant .
` Is it true what I hear of thee , that thou canst burnish sword ? '
` It be true , ' answer Kai .
Then be the sword of Gwrnach bring to him .
` Shall it be burnish white or blue ? '
say Kai , take a whetstone from under his arm .
` As thou wilt , ' answer the giant , and speedily do Kai polish half the sword .
The giant marvel at his skill , and say : ` It be a wonder that such a man a thou shouldst be without a companion . ' '
I have a companion , noble sir , but he have no skill in this art . '
` What be his name ? '
ask the giant .
` Let the porter go forth , and I will tell him how he may know him .
The head of his lance will leave it shaft , and draw blood from the wind , and descend upon it shaft again . '
So the porter open the gate and Bedwyr enter .
Now there be much talk amongst those who remain without when the gate close upon Bedwyr , and Goreu , son of Custennin , prevail with the porter , and he and his companion get in also and hide themselves .
By this time the whole of the sword be polish , and Kai give it into the hand of Gwrnach the giant , who felt it and say : ` Thy work be good ; I be content . '
Then say Kai : ` It be thy scabbard that hath rust thy sword ; give it to me that I may take out the wooden side of it and put in new one . '
And he take the scabbard in one hand and the sword in the other , and come and stand behind the giant , a if he would have sheathe the sword in the scabbard .
But with it he strike a blow at the head of the giant , and it roll from his body .
After that they despoil the castle of it gold and jewel , and return , bear the sword of the giant , to Arthur 's court .
They tell Arthur how they have speed , and they all take counsel together , and agree that they must set out on the quest for Mabon the son of Modron , and Gwrhyr , who know the language of beast and of bird , go with them .
SO they journey until they come to the nest of an ousel , and Gwrhyr speak to her .
` Tell me if thou knowest aught of Mabon the son of Modron , who be take when three night old from between his mother and the wall . '
And the ousel answer : ` When I first come here I be a young bird , and there be a smith 's anvil in this place .
But from that time no work have be do upon it , save that every evening I have peck at it , till now there be not so much a the size of a nut remain thereof .
Yet all that time I have never once heard of the man you name .
Still , there be a race of beast old than I , and I will guide you to them . '
So the ousel fly before them , till she reach the stag of Redynvre ; but when they inquire of the stag whether he know aught of Mabon he shake his head .
` When first I come hither , ' say he , ` the plain be bare save for one oak sapling , which grow up to be an oak with a hundred branch .
All that be leave of that oak be a withered stump , but never once have I heard of the man you name .
Nevertheless , a you be Arthur 's men , I will guide you to the place where there be an animal old than I ' ; and the stag run before them till he reach the owl of Cwm Cawlwyd .
But when they inquire of the owl if he know aught of Mabon he shake his head .
` When first I come hither , ' say he , ` the valley be a wooded glen ; then a race of men come and root it up .
After that there grow a second wood , and then a third , which you see .
Look at my wing also -- be they not wither stump ?
Yet until to-day I have never hear of the man you name .
Still , I will guide you to the old animal in the world , and the one that have travel most , the eagle of Gwern Abbey . '
And he fly before them , as fast a his old wing would carry him , till he reach the eagle of Gwern Abbey , but when they inquire of the eagle whether he know aught of Mabon he shake his head .
` When I first come hither , ' say the eagle , ` there be a rock here , and every evening I peck at the star from the top of it .
Now , behold , it be not even a span high !
But only once have I heard of the man you name , and that be when I go in search of food as far a Llyn Llyw .
I swoop down upon a salmon , and strike my claw into him , but he draw me down under water till scarcely could I escape him .
Then I summon all my kindred to destroy him , but he make peace with me , and I take fifty fish spear from his back .
Unless he may know something of the man whom you seek I can not tell who may .
But I will guide you to the place where he be . '
So they follow the eagle , who fly before them , though so high be he in the sky , it be often hard to mark his flight .
At length he stop above a deep pool in a river .
` Salmon of Llyn Llyw , ' he call , ' I have come to thee with an embassy from Arthur to inquire if thou knowest aught concern Mabon the son of Modron . '
And the salmon answer : ` As much a I know I will tell thee .
With every tide I go up the river , till I reach the wall of Gloucester , and there have I find such wrong a I never find elsewhere .
And that you may see that what I say be true let two of you go thither on my shoulder . '
So Kai and Gwrhyr go upon the shoulder of the salmon , and be carry under the wall of the prison , from which proceed the sound of great weeping .
` Who be it that thus lament in this house of stone ? '
` It be I , Mabon the son of Modron . '
` Will silver or gold bring thy freedom , or only battle and fight ? '
ask Gwrhyr again .
` By fight alone shall I be set free , ' say Mabon .
Then they send a messenger to Arthur to tell him that Mabon be find , and he bring all his warrior to the castle of Gloucester and fell fiercely upon it ; while Kai and Bedwyr go on the shoulder of the salmon to the gate of the dungeon , and break it down and carry away Mabon .
And he now be free return home with Arthur .
After this , on a certain day , a Gwythyr be walk across a mountain he hear a grievous cry , and he hasten towards it .
In a little valley he saw the heather burning and the fire spread fast towards the anthill , and all the ant be hurry to and fro , not know whither to go .
Gwythyr have pity on them , and put out the fire , and in gratitude the ant bring him the nine bushel of flax seed which Yspaddaden Penkawr require of Kilweh .
And many of the other marvel be do likewise by Arthur and his knight , and at last it come to the fight with Trwyth the board , to obtain the comb and the scissors and the razor that lay between his ear .
But hard be the boar to catch , and fiercely do he fight when Arthur 's men give him battle , so that many of them be slain .
Up and down the country go Trwyth the boar , and Arthur follow after him , till they come to the Severn sea .
There three knight catch his foot unawares and plunge him into the water , while one snatch the razor from him , and another seize the scissors .
But before they lay hold of the comb he have shake them all off , and neither man nor horse nor dog could reach him till he come to Cornwall , whither Arthur have swear he should not go .
Thither Arthur follow after him with his knight , and if it have be hard to win the razor and the scissors , the struggle for the comb be fiercer still , but at length Arthur prevail , and the boar be drive into the sea .
And whether he be drown or where he go no man know to this day .
In the end all the marvel be do , and Kilweh set forward , and with him Goreu , the son of Custennin , to Yspaddaden Penkawr , bear in their hand the razor , the scissors and the comb , and Yspaddaden Penkawr be shave by Kaw .
` Is thy daughter mine now ? '
ask Kilweh .
` She be thine , ' answer Yspaddaden , ` but it be Arthur and none other who have win her for thee .
Of my own free will thou shouldst never have have her , for now I must lose my life . '
And a he spake Goreu the son of Custennin cut off his head , a if have be ordain , and Arthur 's host return each man to his own country .
