THE ORANGE FAIRY BOOK The Story of the Hero Makoma From the Senna -LRB- Oral Tradition -RRB- Once upon a time , at the town of Senna on the bank of the Zambesi , be bear a child .
He be not like other child , for he be very tall and strong ; over his shoulder he carry a big sack , and in his hand an iron hammer .
He could also speak like a grown man , but usually he be very silent .
One day his mother say to him : ` My child , by what name shall we know you ? '
And he answer : ` Call all the head men of Senna here to the river 's bank . '
And his mother call the head men of the town , and when they have come he lead them down to a deep black pool in the river where all the fierce crocodile live . '
O great men ! '
he say , while they all listen , ` which of you will leap into the pool and overcome the crocodile ? '
But no one would come forward .
So he turn and spring into the water and disappear .
The people hold their breath , for they think : ` Surely the boy be bewitch and throw away his life , for the crocodile will eat him ! '
Then suddenly the ground tremble , and the pool , heave and swirl , become red with blood , and presently the boy rise to the surface swam on shore .
But he be no longer just a boy !
He be strong than any man and very tall and handsome , so that the people shout with gladness when they saw him .
` Now , O my people ! '
he cry , wave his hand , ` you know my name -- I be Makoma , `` the Greater '' ; for have I not slay the crocodile into the pool where none would venture ? '
Then he say to his mother : ` Rest gently , my mother , for I go to make a home for myself and become a hero . '
Then , enter his hut he take Nu-endo , his iron hammer , and throw the sack over his shoulder , he go away .
Makoma cross the Zambesi , and for many moon he wander towards the north and west until he come to a very hilly country where , one day , he meet a huge giant making mountain .
` Greeting , ' shout Makoma , ` you be you ? ' '
I be Chi-eswa-mapiri , who make the mountain , ' answer the giant ; ` and who be you ? ' '
I be Makoma , which signify `` great , '' ' answer he .
` Greater than who ? '
ask the giant .
` Greater than you ! '
answer Makoma .
The giant give a roar and rush upon him .
Makoma say nothing , but swing his great hammer , Nu-endo , he strike the giant upon the head .
He strike him so hard a blow that the giant shrank into quite a little man , who fell upon his knee say : ` You be indeed great than I , O Makoma ; take me with you to be your slave ! '
So Makoma pick him up and drop him into the sack that he carry upon his back .
He be great than ever now , for all the giant 's strength have go into him ; and he resume his journey , carry his burden with as little difficulty a an eagle might carry a hare .
Before long he come to a country break up with huge stone and immense clod of earth .
Looking over one of the heap he saw a giant wrapped in dust drag out the very earth and hurl it in handful on either side of him .
` Who be you , ' cried Makoma , ` that pull up the earth in this way ? ' '
I be Chi-dubula-taka , ' say he , ` and I be make the river-beds . '
` Do you know who I be ? '
say Makoma . '
I be he that be call `` great '' ! '
` Greater than who ? '
thunder the giant .
` Greater than you ! '
answer Makoma .
With a shout , Chi-dubula-taka seize a great clod of earth and launch it at Makoma .
But the hero have his sack hold over his left arm and the stone and earth fell harmlessly upon it , and , tightly grip his iron hammer , he rush in and strike the giant to the ground .
Chi-dubula-taka grovel before him , all the while grow small and small ; and when he have become a convenient size Makoma pick him up and put him into the sack beside Chi-eswa-mapiri .
He go on his way even great than before , a all the river-maker 's power have become his ; and at last he come to a forest of bao-babs and thorn tree .
He be astonish at their size , for every one be full grown and large than any tree he have ever see , and close by he saw Chi-gwisa-miti , the giant who be plant the forest .
Chi-gwisa-miti be tall than either of his brother , but Makoma be not afraid , and call out to him : ` Who be you , O Big One ? '
` I , ' say the giant , ` be Chi-gwisa-miti , and I be plant these bao-babs and thorn a food for my child the elephant . '
` Leave off ! '
shout the hero , ` for I be Makoma , and would like to exchange a blow with thee ! '
The giant , pluck up a monster bao-bab by the root , strike heavily at Makoma ; but the hero sprang aside , and a the weapon sink deep into the soft earth , whirl Nu-endo the hammer round his head and fell the giant with one blow .
So terrible be the stroke that Chi-gwisa-miti shrivel up a the other giant have do ; and when he have get back his breath he beg Makoma to take him a his servant .
` For , ' say he , ` it be honourable to serve a man so great a thou . '
Makoma , after place him in his sack , proceed upon his journey , and travel for many day he at last reach a country so barren and rocky that not a single living thing grow upon it -- everywhere reign grim desolation .
And in the midst of this dead region he find a man eat fire .
` What be you do ? '
demand Makoma . '
I be eat fire , ' answer the man , laugh ; ` and my name be Chi-idea-moto , for I be the flame-spirit , and can waste and destroy what I like . '
` You be wrong , ' say Makoma ; ` for I be Makoma , who be `` great '' than you -- and you can not destroy me ! '
The fire-eater laugh again , and blow a flame at Makoma .
But the hero sprang behind a rock -- just in time , for the ground upon which he have be stand be turn to melt glass , like an overbaked pot , by the heat of the flame-spirit 's breath .
Then the hero fling his iron hammer at Chi-idea-moto , and , strike him , it knock him helpless ; so Makoma place him in the sack , Woro-nowu , with the other great men that he have overcome .
And now , truly , Makoma be a very great hero ; for he have the strength to make hill , the industry to lead river over dry waste , foresight and wisdom in plant tree , and the power of produce fire when he wish .
Wandering on he arrive one day at a great plain , well water and full of game ; and in the very middle of it , close to a large river , be a grassy spot , very pleasant to make a home upon .
Makoma be so delighted with the little meadow that he sit down under a large tree and remove the sack from his shoulder , take out all the giant and set them before him .
` My friend , ' say he , ' I have travel far and be weary .
Is not this such a place a would suit a hero for his home ?
Let u then go , to-morrow , to bring in timber to make a kraal . '
So the next day Makoma and the giant set out to get pole to build the kraal , leave only Chi-eswa-mapiri to look after the place and cook some venison which they have kill .
In the evening , when they return , they find the giant helpless and tie to a tree by one enormous hair !
` How be it , ' say Makoma , astonish , ` that we find you thus bind and helpless ? ' '
O Chief , ' answer Chi-eswa-mapiri , ` at mid-day a man come out of the river ; he be of immense statue , and his grey moustache be of such length that I could not see where they end !
He demand of me `` Who be thy master ? ''
And I answer : `` Makoma , the great of hero . ''
Then the man seize me , and pull a hair from his moustache , tie me to this tree -- even a you see me . '
Makoma be very wroth , but he say nothing , and draw his finger-nail across the hair -LRB- which be a thick and strong a palm rope -RRB- cut it , and set free the mountain-maker .
The three follow day exactly the same thing happen , only each time with a different one of the party ; and on the fourth day Makoma stay in camp when the others go to cut pole , say that he would see for himself what sort of man this be that live in the river and whose moustache be so long that they extend beyond men 's sight .
So when the giant have go he sweep and tidy the camp and put some venison on the fire to roast .
At midday , when the sun be right overhead , he hear a rumble noise from the river , and look up he saw the head and shoulder of an enormous man emerge from it .
And behold !
right down the river-bed and up the river-bed , till they fade into the blue distance , stretch the giant 's grey moustache !
` Who be you ? '
bellow the giant , as soon a he be out of the water . '
I be he that be call Makoma , ' answer the hero ; ` and , before I slay thee , tell me also what be thy name and what thou doest in the river ? '
` My name be Chin-debou Mau-giri , ' say the giant .
` My home be in the river , for my moustache be the grey fever-mist that hang above the water , and with which I bind all those that come unto me so that they die . '
` You can not bind me ! '
shout Makoma , rush upon him and strike with his hammer .
But the river giant be so slimy that the blow slide harmlessly off his green chest , and a Makoma stumble and try to regain his balance , the giant swung one of his long hair around him and trip him up .
For a moment Makoma be helpless , but remember the power of the flame-spirit which have enter into him , he breathe a fiery breath upon the giant 's hair and cut himself free .
As Chin-debou Mau-giri lean forward to seize him the hero fling his sack Woronowu over the giant 's slippery head , and grip his iron hammer , strike him again ; this time the blow alight upon the dry sack and Chin-debou Mau-giri fell dead .
When the four giant return at sunset with the pole , they rejoice to find that Makoma have overcome the fever-spirit , and they feast on the roast venison till far into the night ; but in the morning , when they awake , Makoma be already warm his hand to the fire , and his face be gloomy .
` In the darkness of the night , O my friend , ' he say presently , ` the white spirit of my father come upon me and speak , say : `` Get thee hence , Makoma , for thou shalt have no rest until thou hast find and fought with Sakatirina , who have five head , and be very great and strong ; so take leave of thy friend , for thou must go alone . '' '
Then the giant be very sad , and bewail the loss of their hero ; but Makoma comfort them , and give back to each the gift he have take from them .
Then bid them ` Farewell , ' he go on his way .
Makoma travel far towards the west ; over rough mountain and water-logged morass , ford deep river , and tramp for day across dry desert where most men would have die , until at length he arrive at a hut stand near some large peak , and inside the hut be two beautiful woman .
` Greeting ! '
say the hero .
` Is this the country of Sakatirina of five head , whom I be seek ? '
` We greet you , O Great One ! '
answer the woman .
` We be the wife of Sakatirina ; your search be at an end , for there stand he whom you seek ! '
And they point to what Makoma have think be two tall mountain peak .
` Those be his leg , ' they say ; ` his body you can not see , for it be hide in the cloud . '
Makoma be astonish when he behold how tall be the giant ; but , nothing daunt , he go forward until he reach one of Sakatirina 's leg , which he strike heavily with Nu-endo .
Nothing happen , so he hit again and then again until , presently , he hear a tired , far-away voice say : ` Who be it that scratch my foot ? '
And Makoma shout as loud a he could , answer : ` It be I , Makoma , who be call `` Greater '' ! '
And he listen , but there be no answer .
Then Makoma collect all the dead brushwood and tree that he could find , and make an enormous pile round the giant 's leg , set a light to it .
This time the giant spoke ; his voice be very terrible , for it be the rumble of thunder in the cloud .
` Who be it , ' he say , ` make that fire smoulder around my foot ? '
` It be I , Makoma ! '
shout the hero .
` And I have come from far away to see thee , O Sakatirina , for the spirit of my father bid me go seek and fight with thee , l I should grow fat , and weary of myself . '
There be silence for a while , and then the giant speak softly : ` It be good , O Makoma ! '
he say .
` For I too have grow weary .
There be no man so great a I , therefore I be all alone .
Guard thyself ! '
and bend suddenly he seize the hero in his hand and dash him upon the ground .
And lo !
instead of death , Makoma have find life , for he spring to his foot mightier in strength and stature than before , and rush in he grip the giant by the waist and wrestle with him .
Hour by hour they fight , and mountain roll beneath their foot like pebble in a flood ; now Makoma would break away , and summon up his strength , strike the giant with Nu-endo his iron hammer , and Sakatirina would pluck up the mountain and hurl them upon the hero , but neither one could slay the other .
At last , upon the second day , they grapple so strongly that they could not break away ; but their strength be fail , and , just a the sun be sink , they fell together to the ground , insensible .
In the morning when they awake , Mulimo the Great Spirit be stand by them ; and he say : ' O Makoma and Sakatirina !
Ye be hero so great that no man may come against you .
Therefore ye will leave the world and take up your home with me in the cloud . '
And a he spake the hero become invisible to the people of the Earth , and be no more see among them .
The Magic Mirror -LSB- Native Rhodesian Tale . -RSB-
From the Senna A long , long while ago , before ever the White Men be see in Senna , there live a man call Gopani-Kufa .
One day , a he be out hunting , he come upon a strange sight .
An enormous python have catch an antelope and coil itself around it ; the antelope , strike out in despair with it horn , have pin the python 's neck to a tree , and so deeply have it horn sunk in the soft wood that neither creature could get away .
` Help ! '
cry the antelope , ` for I be do no harm , yet I have be catch , and would have be eat , have I not defend myself . '
` Help me , ' say the python , ` for I be Insato , King of all the Reptiles , and will reward you well ! '
Gopani-Kufa consider for a moment , then stab the antelope with his assegai , he set the python free . '
I thank you , ' say the python ; ` come back here with the new moon , when I shall have eat the antelope , and I will reward you a I promise . '
` Yes , ' say the die antelope , ` he will reward you , and lo !
your reward shall be your own undoing ! '
Gopani-Kufa go back to his kraal , and with the new moon he return again to the spot where he have save the python .
Insato be lie upon the ground , still sleepy from the effect of his huge meal , and when he saw the man he thank him again , and say : ` Come with me now to Pita , which be my own country , and I will give you what you will of all my possession . '
Gopani-Kufa at first be afraid , thinking of what the antelope have say , but finally he consent and follow Insato into the forest .
For several day they travel , and at last they come to a hole lead deep into the earth .
It be not very wide , but large enough to admit a man .
` Hold on to my tail , ' say Insato , ` and I will go down first , draw you after me . '
The man do so , and Insato enter .
Down , down , down they go for day , all the while get deep and deeply into the earth , until at last the darkness end and they drop into a beautiful country ; around them grow short green grass , on which browse herd of cattle and sheep and goat .
In the distance Gopani-Kufa saw a great collection of house all square , built of stone and very tall , and their roof be shin with gold and burnish iron .
Gopani-Kufa turn to Insato , but find , in the place of the python , a man , strong and handsome , with the great snake 's skin wrap round him for cover ; and on his arm and neck be ring of pure gold .
The man smile . '
I be Insato , ' say he , ` but in my own country I take man 's shape -- even a you see me -- for this be Pita , the land over which I be king . '
He then take Gopani-Kufa by the hand and lead him towards the town .
On the way they pass river in which men and woman be bath and fishing and boating ; and farther on they come to garden cover with heavy crop of rice and maize , and many other grain which Gopani-Kufa do not even know the name of .
And a they pass , the people who be sing at their work in the field , abandon their labour and salute Insato with delight , bring also palm wine and green cocoanut for refreshment , a to one return from a long journey .
` These be my child ! '
say Insato , wave his hand towards the people .
Gopani-Kufa be much astonish at all that he saw , but he say nothing .
Presently they come to the town ; everything here , too , be beautiful , and everything that a man might desire he could obtain .
Even the grain of dust in the street be of gold and silver .
Insato conduct Gopani-Kufa to the palace , and show him his room , and the maiden who would wait upon him , tell him that they would have a great feast that night , and on the morrow he might name his choice of the rich of Pita and it should be give him .
Then he be away .
Now Gopani-Kufa have a wasp call Zengi-mizi .
Zengi-mizi be not an ordinary wasp , for the spirit of the father of Gopani-Kufa have enter it , so that it be exceedingly wise .
In time of doubt Gopani-Kufa always consult the wasp a to what have good be do , so on this occasion he take it out of the little rush basket in which he carry it , say : ` Zengi-mizi , what gift shall I ask of Insato to-morrow when he would know the reward he shall bestow on me for save his life ? '
` Biz-z-z , ' hum Zengi-mizi , ` ask him for Sipao the Mirror . '
And it fly back into it basket .
Gopani-Kufa be astonish at this answer ; but know that the word of Zengi-mizi be true word , he determine to make the request .
So that night they feast , and on the morrow Insato come to Gopani-Kufa and , give him greet joyfully , he say : ` Now , O my friend , name your choice amongst my possession and you shall have it ! ' '
O king ! '
answer Gopani-Kufa , ` out of all your possession I will have the Mirror , Sipao . '
The king start . '
O friend , Gopani-Kufa , ' he say , ` ask anything but that !
I do not think that you would request that which be most precious to me . '
` Let me think over it again then , O king , ' say Gopani-Kufa , ` and to-morrow I will let you know if I change my mind . '
But the king be still much trouble , fear the loss of Sipao , for the mirror have magic power , so that he who own it have but to ask and his wish would be fulfil ; to it Insato owe all that he possess .
As soon a the king leave him , Gopani-Kufa again take Zengi-mizi , out of his basket .
` Zengi-mizi , ' he say , ` the king seem loth to grant my request for the Mirror -- be there not some other thing of equal value for which I might ask ? '
And the wasp answer : ` There be nothing in the world , O Gopani-Kufa , which be of such value a this Mirror , for it be a Wishing Mirror , and accomplish the desire of him who own it .
If the king hesitates , go to him the next day , and the day after , and in the end he will bestow the Mirror upon you , for you save his life . '
And it be even so .
For three day Gopani-Kufa return the same answer to the king , and , at last , with tear in his eye , Insato give him the Mirror , which be of polished iron , say : ` Take Sipao , then , O Gopani-Kufa , and may thy wish come true .
Go back now to thine own country ; Sipao will show you the way . '
Gopani-Kufa be greatly rejoice , and , take farewell of the king , say to the Mirror : ` Sipao , Sipao , I wish to be back upon the Earth again ! '
Instantly he find himself stand upon the upper earth ; but , not know the spot , he say again to the Mirror : ` Sipao , Sipao , I want the path to my own kraal ! '
And behold !
right before him lay the path !
When he arrive home he find his wife and daughter mourn for him , for they think that he have be eat by lion ; but he comfort them , say that while follow a wounded antelope he have miss his way and have wander for a long time before he have find the path again .
That night he ask Zengi-mizi , in whom sit the spirit of his father , what he have well ask Sipao for next ?
` Biz-z-z , ' say the wasp , ` would you not like to be as great a chief a Insato ? '
And Gopani-Kufa smile , and take the Mirror and say to it : ` Sipao , Sipao , I want a town as great a that of Insato , the King of Pita ; and I wish to be chief over it ! '
Then all along the bank of the Zambesi river , which flow near by , spring up street of stone building , and their roof shone with gold and burnish iron like those in Pita ; and in the street men and woman be walk , and young boy be drive out the sheep and cattle to pasture ; and from the river come shout and laughter from the young men and maiden who have launch their canoe and be fish .
And when the people of the new town behold Gopani-Kufa they rejoice greatly and hail him a chief .
Gopani-Kufa be now as powerful a Insato the King of the Reptiles have be , and he and his family move into the palace that stand high above the other building right in the middle of the town .
His wife be too astonish at all these wonder to ask any question , but his daughter Shasasa keep beg him to tell her how he have suddenly become so great ; so at last he reveal the whole secret , and even entrust Sipao the Mirror to her care , say : ` It will be safer with you , my daughter , for you dwell apart ; whereas men come to consult me on affair of state , and the Mirror might be steal . '
Then Shasasa take the Magic Mirror and hide it beneath her pillow , and after that for many year Gopani-Kufa rule his people both well and wisely , so that all men love him , and never once do he need to ask Sipao to grant him a wish .
Now it happen that , after many year , when the hair of Gopani-Kufa be turn grey with age , there come white men to that country .
Up the Zambesi they come , and they fight long and fiercely with Gopani-Kufa ; but , because of the power of the Magic Mirror , he beat them , and they flee to the sea-coast .
Chief among them be one Rei , a man of much cunning , who seek to discover whence sprang Gopani-Kufa 's power .
So one day he call to him a trusty servant name Butou , and say : ` Go you to the town and find out for me what be the secret of it greatness . '
And Butou , dress himself in rag , set out , and when he come to Gopani-Kufa 's town he ask for the chief ; and the people take him into the presence of Gopani-Kufa .
When the white man saw him he humble himself , and say : ' O Chief !
take pity on me , for I have no home !
When Rei march against you I alone stand apart , for I know that all the strength of the Zambesi lay in your hand , and because I would not fight against you he turn me forth into the forest to starve ! '
And Gopani-Kufa believe the white man 's story , and he take him in and feast him , and give him a house .
In this way the end come .
For the heart of Shasasa , the daughter of Gopani-Kufa , go forth to Butou the traitor , and from her he learn the secret of the Magic Mirror .
One night , when all the town slept , he felt beneath her pillow and , find the Mirror , he steal it and flee back with it to Rei , the chief of the white men .
So it befall that , one day , a Gopani-Kufa be gaze up at the river from a window of the palace he again saw the war-canoes of the white men ; and at the sight his spirit misgive him .
` Shasasa !
my daughter ! '
he cry wildly , ` go fetch me the mirror , for the white men be at hand . '
` Woe be me , my father ! '
she sob .
` The Mirror be go !
For I love Butou the traitor , and he have steal Sipao from me ! '
Then Gopani-Kufa calm himself , and draw out Zengi-mizi from it rush basket . '
O spirit of my father ! '
he say , ` what now shall I do ? ' '
O Gopani-Kufa ! '
hum the wasp , ` there be nothing now that can be do , for the word of the antelope which you slew be be fulfil . '
` Alas !
I be an old man -- I have forget ! '
cry the chief .
` The word of the antelope be true word -- my reward shall be my undo -- they be be fulfil ! '
Then the white men fell upon the people of Gopani-Kufa and slew them together with the chief and his daughter Shasasa ; and since then all the power of the Earth have rest in the hand of the white men , for they have in their possession Sipao , the Magic Mirror .
Story of the King Who Would See Paradise Once upon a time there be king who , one day out hunting , come upon a fakeer in a lonely place in the mountain .
The fakeer be seat on a little old bedstead read the Koran , with his patched cloak throw over his shoulder .
The king ask him what he be read ; and he say he be read about Paradise , and pray that he might be worthy to enter there .
Then they begin to talk , and , by-and-bye , the king ask the fakeer if he could show him a glimpse of Paradise , for he find it very difficult to believe in what he could not see .
The fakeer reply that he be ask a very difficult , and perhaps a very dangerous , thing ; but that he would pray for him , and perhaps he might be able to do it ; only he warn the king both against the danger of his unbelief , and against the curiosity which prompt him to ask this thing .
However , the king be not to be turn from his purpose , and he promise the fakeer always to provide him with food , if he , in return , would pray for him .
To this the fakeer agree , and so they part .
Time go on , and the king always send the old fakeer his food accord to his promise ; but , whenever he send to ask him when he be go to show him Paradise , the fakeer always reply : ` Not yet , not yet ! '
After a year or two have pass by , the king heard one day that the fakeer be very ill -- indeed , he be believe to be die .
Instantly he hurry off himself , and find that it be really true , and that the fakeer be even then breathe his last .
There and then the king beseech him to remember his promise , and to show him a glimpse of Paradise .
The die fakeer reply that if the king would come to his funeral , and , when the grave be fill in , and everyone else be go away , he would come and lay his hand upon the grave , he would keep his word , and show him a glimpse of Paradise .
At the same time he implore the king not to do this thing , but to be content to see Paradise when God call him there .
Still the king 's curiosity be so aroused that he would not give way .
Accordingly , after the fakeer be dead , and have be bury , he stay behind when all the rest go away ; and then , when he be quite alone , he step forward , and lay his hand upon the grave !
Instantly the ground open , and the astonished king , peep in , saw a flight of rough step , and , at the bottom of them , the fakeer sitting , just a he use to sit , on his rickety bedstead , read the Koran !
At first the king be so surprised and frighten that he could only stare ; but the fakeer beckon to him to come down , so , muster up his courage , he boldly step down into the grave .
The fakeer rise , and , make a sign to the king to follow , walk a few pace along a dark passage .
Then he stop , turn solemnly to his companion , and , with a movement of his hand , draw aside a it be a heavy curtain , and reveal -- what ?
No one know what be there show to the king , nor do he ever tell anyone ; but , when the fakeer at length drop the curtain , and the king turn to leave the place , he have have his glimpse of Paradise !
Trembling in every limb , he stagger back along the passage , and stumble up the step out of the tomb into the fresh air again .
The dawn be break .
It seem odd to the king that he have be so long in the grave .
It appear but a few minute ago that he have descend , pass along a few step to the place where he have peep beyond the veil , and return again after perhaps five minute of that wonderful view !
And what WAS it he have see ?
He rack his brain to remember , but he could not call to mind a single thing !
How curious everything look too !
Why , his own city , which by now he be enter , seem change and strange to him !
The sun be already up when he turn into the palace gate and enter the public durbar hall .
It be full ; a chamberlain come across and ask him why he sit unbidden in the king 's presence .
` But I be the king ! '
he cry .
` What king ? '
say the chamberlain .
` The true king of this country , ' say he indignantly .
Then the chamberlain go away , and speak to the king who sit on the throne , and the old king heard word like ` mad , ' ` age , ' ` compassion . '
Then the king on the throne call him to come forward , and , a he go , he catch sight of himself reflect in the polished steel shield of the bodyguard , and start back in horror !
He be old , decrepit , dirty , and rag !
His long white beard and lock be unkempt , and straggle all over his chest and shoulder .
Only one sign of royalty remain to him , and that be the signet ring upon his right hand .
He drag it off with shake finger and hold it up to the king .
` Tell me who I be , ' he cry ; ` there be my signet , who once sit where you sit -- even yesterday ! '
The king look at him compassionately , and examine the signet with curiosity .
Then he command , and they bring out dusty record and archive of the kingdom , and old coin of previous reign , and compare them faithfully .
At last the king turn to the old man , and say : ` Old man , such a king a this whose signet thou hast , reign seven hundred year ago ; but he be say to have disappear , none know whither ; where get you the ring ? '
Then the old man smite his breast , and cry out with a loud lamentation ; for he understand that he , who be not content to wait patiently to see the Paradise of the faithful , have be judge already .
And he turn and leave the hall without a wor , and go into the jungle , where he live for twenty-five year a life of prayer and and meditation , until at last the Angel of Death come to him , and mercifully release him , purge and purify through his punishment .
How Isuro the Rabbit Tricked Gudu -LSB- A Pathan story tell to Major Campbell . -RSB-
Far away in a hot country , where the forest be very thick and dark , and the river very swift and strong , there once live a strange pair of friend .
Now one of the friend be a big white rabbit name Isuro , and the other be a tall baboon call Gudu , and so fond be they of each other that they be seldom see apart .
One day , when the sun be hotter even than usual , the rabbit awoke from his midday sleep , and saw Gudu the baboon stand beside him .
` Get up , ' say Gudu ; ' I be go courting , and you must come with me .
So put some food in a bag , and sling it round your neck , for we may not be able to find anything to eat for a long while . '
Then the rabbit rub his eye , and gather a store of fresh green thing from under the bush , and tell Gudu that he be ready for the journey .
They go on quite happily for some distance , and at last they come to a river with rock scatter here and there across the stream .
` We can never jump those wide space if we be burden with food , ' say Gudu , ` we must throw it into the river , unless we wish to fall in ourselves . '
And stoop down , unseen by Isuro , who be in front of him , Gudu pick up a big stone , and throw it into the water with a loud splash .
` It be your turn now , ' he cry to Isuro .
And with a heavy sigh , the rabbit unfasten his bag of food , which fell into the river .
The road on the other side lead down an avenue of tree , and before they have go very far Gudu open the bag that lay hide in the thick hair about his neck , and begin to eat some delicious-looking fruit .
` Where do you get that from ? '
ask Isuro enviously .
` Oh , I find after all that I could get across the rock quite easily , so it seem a pity not to keep my bag , ' answer Gudu .
` Well , a you trick me into throw away mine , you ought to let me share with you , ' say Isuro .
But Gudu pretend not to hear him , and strode along the path .
By-and-bye they enter a wood , and right in front of them be a tree so laden with fruit that it branch sweep the ground .
And some of the fruit be still green , and some yellow .
The rabbit hop forward with joy , for he be very hungry ; but Gudu say to him : ` Pluck the green fruit , you will find it much the best .
I will leave it all for you , a you have have no dinner , and take the yellow for myself . '
So the rabbit take one of the green orange and begin to bite it , but it skin be so hard that he could hardly get his teeth through the rind .
` It do not taste at all nice , ' he cry , screw up his face ; ' I would rather have one of the yellow one . '
` No !
no !
I really could not allow that , ' answer Gudu .
` They would only make you ill .
Be content with the green fruit . '
And a they be all he could get , Isuro be force to put up with them .
After this have happen two or three time , Isuro at last have his eye open , and make up his mind that , whatever Gudu tell him , he would do exactly the opposite .
However , by this time they have reach the village where dwelt Gudu 's future wife , and a they enter Gudu point to a clump of bush , and say to Isuro : ` Whenever I be eat , and you hear me call out that my food have burn me , run as fast a you can and gather some of those leaf that they may heal my mouth . '
The rabbit would have like to ask him why he eat food that he know would burn him , only he be afraid , and just nod in reply ; but when they have go on a little further , he say to Gudu : ' I have drop my needle ; wait here a moment while I go and fetch it . '
` Be quick then , ' answer Gudu , climb into a tree .
And the rabbit hasten back to the bush , and gather a quantity of the leaf , which he hide among his fur , ` For , ' think he , ` if I get them now I shall save myself the trouble of a walk by-and-by . '
When he have pluck as many a he want he return to Gudu , and they go on together .
The sun be almost setting by the time they reach their journey 's end and be very tire they gladly sit down by a well .
Then Gudu 's betroth , who have be watch for him , bring out a pitcher of water -- which she pour over them to wash off the dust of the road -- and two portion of food .
But once again the rabbit 's hope be dash to the ground , for Gudu say hastily : ` The custom of the village forbids you to eat till I have finish . '
And Isuro do not know that Gudu be lie , and that he only want more food .
So he saw hungrily look on , wait till his friend have have enough .
In a little while Gudu scream loudly : ' I be burnt !
I be burnt ! '
though he be not burn at all .
Now , though Isuro have the leaf about him , he do not dare to produce them at the last moment lest the baboon should guess why he have stay behind .
So he just go round a corner for a short time , and then come hop back in a great hurry .
But , quick though he be , Gudu have be quicker still , and nothing remain but some drop of water .
` How unlucky you be , ' say Gudu , snatch the leaf ; ` no sooner have you go than ever so many people arrive , and wash their hand , a you see , and eat your portion . '
But , though Isuro know good than to believe him , he say nothing , and go to bed hungry than he have ever be in his life .
Early next morning they start for another village , and pass on the way a large garden where people be very busy gather monkey-nuts .
` You can have a good breakfast at last , ' say Gudu , point to a heap of empty shell ; never doubt but that Isuro would meekly take the portion show him , and leave the real nut for himself .
But what be his surprise when Isuro answer : ` Thank you ; I think I should prefer these . '
And , turn to the kernel , never stop as long a there be one left .
And the bad of it be that , with so many people about , Gudu could not take the nut from him .
It be night when they reach the village where dwell the mother of Gudu 's betroth , who lay meat and millet porridge before them . '
I think you tell me you be fond of porridge , ' say Gudu ; but Isuro answer : ` You be mistake me for somebody else , a I always eat meat when I can get it . '
And again Gudu be force to be content with the porridge , which he hat .
While he be eat it , however a sudden thought dart into his mind , and he manage to knock over a great pot of water which be hang in front of the fire , and put it quite out .
` Now , ' say the cunning creature to himself , ' I shall be able in the dark to steal his meat ! '
But the rabbit have grow as cunning a he , and stand in a corner hide the meat behind him , so that the baboon could not find it . '
O Gudu ! '
he cry , laugh aloud , ` it be you who have teach me to be clever . '
And call to the people of the house , he bid them kindle the fire , for Gudu would sleep by it , but that he would pass the night with some friend in another hut .
It be still quite dark when Isuro hear his name call very softly , and , on open his eye , behold Gudu stand by him .
Laying his finger on his nose , in token of silence , he sign to Isuro to get up and follow him , and it be not until they be some distance from the hut that Gudu speak . '
I be hungry and want something to eat good than that nasty porridge that I have for supper .
So I be go to kill one of those goat , and a you be a good cook you must boil the flesh for me . '
The rabbit nod , and Gudu disappear behind a rock , but soon return drag the dead goat with him .
The two then set about skin it , after which they stuff the skin with dried leaf , so that no one would have guess it be not alive , and set it up in the middle of a lump of bush , which keep it firm on it foot .
While he be do this , Isuro collect stick for a fire , and when it be kindle , Gudu hasten to another hut to steal a pot which he fill with water from the river , and , plant two branch in the ground , they hang the pot with the meat in it over the fire .
` It will not be fit to eat for two hour at least , ' say Gudu , ` so we can both have a nap . '
And he stretch himself out on the ground , and pretend to fall fast asleep , but , in reality , he be only wait till it be safe to take all the meat for himself .
` Surely I hear him snore , ' he think ; and he steal to the place where Isuro be lie on a pile of wood , but the rabbit 's eye be wide open .
` How tiresome , ' mutter Gudu , a he go back to his place ; and after wait a little longer he get up , and peep again , but still the rabbit 's pink eye star widely .
If Gudu have only know , Isuro be asleep all the time ; but this he never guess , and by-and-bye he grow so tired with watch that he go to sleep himself .
Soon after , Isuro wake up , and he too felt hungry , so he creep softly to the pot and eat all the meat , while he tie the bone together and hang them in Gudu 's fur .
After that he go back to the wood-pile and slept again .
In the morning the mother of Gudu 's betroth come out to milk her goat , and on go to the bush where the large one seem entangled , she find out the trick .
She make such lament that the people of the village come run , and Gudu and Isuro jump up also , and pretend to be a surprised and interested a the rest .
But they must have look guilty after all , for suddenly an old man point to them , and cry : ` Those be thief . '
And at the sound of his voice the big Gudu tremble all over .
` How dare you say such thing ?
I defy you to prove it , ' answer Isuro boldly .
And he dance forward , and turn head over heel , and shake himself before them all . '
I speak hastily ; you be innocent , ' say the old man ; ` but now let the baboon do likewise . '
And when Gudu begin to jump the goat 's bone rattle and the people cry : ` It be Gudu who be the goat-slayer ! '
But Gudu answer : ` Nay , I do not kill your goat ; it be Isuro , and he eat the meat , and hang the bone round my neck .
So it be he who should die ! '
And the people look at each other , for they know not what to believe .
At length one man say : ` Let them both die , but they may choose their own death . '
Then Isuro answer : ` If we must die , put u in the place where the wood be cut , and heap it up all round u , so that we can not escape , and set fire to the wood ; and if one be burn and the other be not , then he that be burn be the goat-slayer . '
And the people do a Isuro have say .
But Isuro knew of a hole under the wood-pile , and when the fire be kindle he run into the hole , but Gudu die there .
When the fire have burn itself out and only ash be leave where the wood have be , Isuro come out of his hole , and say to the people : ` Lo !
do I not speak well ?
He who kill your goat be among those ash . '
Ian , the Soldier 's Son -LSB- Mashona Story . -RSB-
There dwell a knight in Grianaig of the land of the West , who have three daughter , and for goodness and beauty they have not their like in all the isle .
All the people love them , and loud be the weeping when one day , a the three maiden sit on the rock on the edge of the sea , dip their foot in the water , there arise a great beast from under the wave and sweep them away beneath the ocean .
And none know whither they have go , or how to seek them .
Now there live in a town a few mile off a soldier who have three son , fine youth and strong , and the best player at shinny in that country .
At Christmastide that year , when family meet together and great feast be hold , Ian , the young of the three brother , say : ` Let u have a match at shinny on the lawn of the knight of Grianaig , for his lawn be wider and the grass smoother than ours . '
But the others answer : ` Nay , for he be in sorrow , and he will think of the game that we have play there when his daughter look on . '
` Let him be please or angry a he will , ' say Ian ; ` we will drive our ball on his lawn to-day . '
And so it be do , and Ian win three game from his brother .
But the knight look out of his window , and be wroth ; and bid his men bring the youth before him .
When he stand in his hall and behold them , his heart be soften somewhat ; but his face be angry a he ask : ` Why do you choose to play shinny in front of my castle when you know full well that the remembrance of my daughter would come back to me ?
The pain which you have make me suffer you shall suffer also . '
` Since we have do you wrong , ' answered Ian , the young , ` build u a ship , and we will go and seek your daughter .
Let them be to windward , or to leeward , or under the four brown boundary of the sea , we will find them before a year and a day go by , and will carry them back to Grianaig . '
In seven day the ship be build , and great store of food and wine place in her .
And the three brother put her head to the sea and sail away , and in seven day the ship run herself on to a beach of white sand , and they all go ashore .
They have none of them ever see that land before , and look about them .
Then they saw that , a short way from them , a number of men be work on a rock , with one man standing over them .
` What place be this ? '
ask the eldest brother .
And the man who be stand by make answer : ` This be the place where dwell the three daughter of the knight of Grianaig , who be to be wed to-morrow to three giant . '
` How can we find them ? '
ask the young man again .
And the overlooker answer : ` To reach the daughter of the knight of Grianaig you must get into this basket , and be draw by a rope up the face of this rock . '
` Oh , that be easily do , ' say the eldest brother , jump into the basket , which at once begin to move -- up , and up , and up -- till he have go about half-way , when a fat black raven fly at him and peck him till he be nearly blind , so that he be force to go back the way he have come .
After that the second brother get into the creel ; but he fare no good , for the raven fly upon him , and he return a his brother have do .
` Now it be my turn , ' say Ian .
But when he be halfway up the raven set upon him also .
` Quick !
quick ! '
cried Ian to the men who hold the rope .
` Quick !
quick !
or I shall be blind ! '
And the men pull with all their might , and in another moment Ian be on top , and the raven behind him .
` Will you give me a piece of tobacco ? '
ask the raven , who be now quite quiet .
` You rascal !
Am I to give you tobacco for try to peck my eye out ? '
answered Ian .
` That be part of my duty , ' reply the raven ; ` but give it to me , and I will prove a good friend to you . '
So Ian break off a piece of tobacco and give it to him .
The raven hide it under his wing , and then go on ; ` Now I will take you to the house of the big giant , where the knight 's daughter sit sew , sew , till even her thimble be wet with tear . '
And the raven hop before him till they reach a large house , the door of which stand open .
They enter and pass through one hall after the other , until they find the knight 's daughter , a the bird have say .
` What bring you here ? '
ask she .
And Ian make answer : ` Why may I not go where you can go ? ' '
I be bring hither by a giant , ' reply she . '
I know that , ' say Ian ; ` but tell me where the giant be , that I may find him . '
` He be on the hunting hill , ' answer she ; ` and nought will bring him home save a shake of the iron chain which hang outside the gate .
But , there , neither to leeward , nor to windward , nor in the four brown boundary of the sea , be there any man that can hold battle against him , save only Ian , the soldier 's son , and he be now but sixteen year old , and how shall he stand against the giant ? '
` In the land whence I have come there be many men with the strength of Ian , ' answer he .
And he go outside and pull at the chain , but he could not move it , and fell on to his knee .
At that he rise swiftly , and gather up his strength , he seize the chain , and this time he shake it so that the link break .
And the giant heard it on the hunting hill , and lift his head , think -- ` It sound like the noise of Ian , the soldier 's son , ' say he ; ` but as yet he be only sixteen year old .
Still , I have well look to it . '
And home he come .
` Are you Ian , the soldier 's son ? '
he ask , a he enter the castle .
` No , of a surety , ' answer the youth , who have no wish that they should know him .
` Then who be you in the leeward , or in the windward , or in the four brown boundary of the sea , who be able to move my battle-chain ? '
` That will be plain to you after wrestle with me a I wrestle with my mother .
And one time she get the good of me , and two time she do not . '
So they wrestle , and twisted and strive with each other till the giant force Ian to his knee .
` You be the strong , ' say Ian ; and the giant answer : ` All men know that ! '
And they take hold of each other once more , and at last Ian throw the giant , and wish that the raven be there to help him .
No sooner have he wish his wish than the raven come .
` Put your hand under my right wing and you will find a knife sharp enough to take off his head , ' say the raven .
And the knife be so sharp that it cut off the giant 's head with a blow .
` Now go and tell the daughter of the king of Grianaig ; but take heed l you listen to her word , and promise to go no far , for she will seek to help you .
Instead , seek the middle daughter , and when you have find her , you shall give me a piece of tobacco for reward . '
` Well have you earn the half of all I have , ' answered Ian .
But the raven shake his head .
` You know only what have pass , and nothing of what lie before .
If you would not fail , wash yourself in clean water , and take balsam from a vessel on top of the door , and rub it over your body , and to-morrow you will be as strong a many men , and I will lead you to the dwelling of the middle one . '
Ian do a the raven bid him , and in spite of the eldest daughter 's entreaty , he set out to seek her next sister .
He find her where she be seat sewing , her very thimble wet from the tear which she have shed .
` What bring you here ? '
ask the second sister .
` Why may I not go where you can go ? '
answered he ; ` and why be you weep ? '
` Because in one day I shall be marry to the giant who be on the hunting hill . '
` How can I get him home ? '
ask Ian .
` Nought will bring him but a shake of that iron chain which hang outside the gate .
But there be neither to leeward , nor to westward , nor in the four brown boundary of the sea , any man that can hold battle with him , save Ian , the soldier 's son , and he be now but sixteen year of age . '
` In the land whence I have come there be many men with the strength of Ian , ' say he .
And he go outside and pull at the chain , but he could not move it , and fell on his knee .
At that he rise to his foot , and gather up his strength mightily , he seize the chain , and this time he shake it so that three link break .
And the second giant heard it on the hunting hill , and lift his head , think -- ` It sound like the noise of Ian , the soldier 's son , ' say he ; ` but as yet he be only sixteen year old .
Still , I have well look to it . '
And home he come .
` Are you Ian , the soldier 's son ? '
he ask , a he enter the castle .
` No , of a surety , ' answer the youth , who have no wish that this giant should know him either ; ` but I will wrestle with you a if I be he . '
Then they seize each other by the shoulder , and the giant throw him on his two knee .
` You be the strong , ' cried Ian ; ` but I be not beat yet . '
And rise to his foot , he throw his arm round the giant .
Backwards and forward they sway , and first one be uppermost and then the other ; but at length Ian work his leg round the giant 's and throw him to the ground .
Then he call to the raven , and the raven come flapping towards him , and say : ` Put your hand under my right wing , and you will find there a knife sharp enough to take off his head . '
And sharp indeed it be , for with a single blow , the giant 's head roll from his body .
` Now wash yourself with warm water , and rub yourself over with oil of balsam , and to-morrow you will be as strong a many men .
But beware of the word of the knight 's daughter , for she be cunning , and will try to keep you at her side .
So farewell ; but first give me a piece of tobacco . '
` That I will gladly , ' answer Ian break off a large bit .
He wash and rub himself that night , a the raven have tell him , and the next morning he enter the chamber where the knight 's daughter be sit .
` Abide here with me , ' she say , ` and be my husband .
There be silver and gold in plenty in the castle . '
But he take no heed , and go on his way till he reach the castle where the knight 's young daughter be sew in the hall .
And tear drop from her eye on to her thimble .
` What bring you here ? '
ask she .
And Ian make answer : ` Why may I not go where you can go ? ' '
I be bring hither by a giant . ' '
I know full well , ' say he .
` Are you Ian , the soldier 's son ? '
ask she again .
And again he answer : ` Yes , I be ; but tell me , why be you weep ? '
` To-morrow the giant will return from the hunting hill , and I must marry him , ' she sob .
And Ian take no heed , and only say : ` How can I bring him home ? '
` Shake the iron chain that hang outside the gate . '
And Ian go out , and give such a pull to the chain that he fell down at full length from the force of the shake .
But in a moment he be on his foot again , and seize the chain with so much strength that four link come off in his hand .
And the giant heard him in the hunting hill , a he be put the game he have kill into a bag .
` In the leeward , or the windward , or in the four brown boundary of the sea , there be none who could give my chain a shake save only Ian , the soldier 's son .
And if he have reach me , then he have leave my two brother dead behind him . '
With that he stride back to the castle , the earth tremble under him a he go .
` Are you Ian , the soldier 's son ? '
ask he .
And the youth answer : ` No , of a surety . '
` Then who be you in the leeward , or the windward , or in the four brown boundary of the sea , who be able to shake my battle chain ?
There be only Ian , the soldier 's son , who can do this , and he be but now sixteen year old . '
I will show you who I be when you have wrestle with me , ' say Ian .
And they throw their arm round each other , and the giant forced Ian on to his knee ; but in a moment he be up again , and crook his leg round the shoulder of the giant , he throw him heavily to the ground .
` Stumpy black raven , come quick ! '
cry he ; and the raven come , and beat the giant about the head with his wing , so that he could not get up .
Then he bid Ian take out a sharp knife from under his feather , which he carry with him for cut berry , and Ian smite off the giant 's head with it .
And so sharp be that knife that , with one blow , the giant 's head roll on the ground .
` Rest now this night also , ' say the raven , ` and to-morrow you shall take the knight 's three daughter to the edge of the rock that lead to the low world .
But take heed to go down first yourself , and let them follow after you .
And before I go you shall give me a piece of tobacco . '
` Take it all , ' answered Ian , ` for well have you earn it . '
` No ; give me but a piece .
You know what be behind you , but you have no knowledge of what be before you . '
And pick up the tobacco in his beak , the raven fly away .
So the next morning the knight 's young daughter load ass with all the silver and gold to be find in the castle , and she set out with Ian the soldier 's son for the house where her second sister be wait to see what would befall .
She also have ass laden with precious thing to carry away , and so have the eldest sister , when they reach the castle where she have be keep a prisoner .
Together they all ride to the edge of the rock , and then Ian lay down and shout , and the basket be draw up , and in it they get one by one , and be let down to the bottom .

Only , he beg the young sister to let him keep the little gold cap which , like the others , she wear on her head ; and then he help them , each in her turn , into the basket .
Long he wait , but wait a he might , the basket never come back , for in their joy at be free the knight 's daughter have forget all about Ian , and have set sail in the ship that have bring him and his brother to the land of Grianaig .
At last he begin to understand what have happen to him , and while he be take counsel with himself what have best be do , the raven come to him .
` You do not heed my word , ' he say gravely .
` No , I do not , and therefore be I here , ' answered Ian , bow his head .
` The past can not be undone , ' go on the raven .
` He that will not take counsel will take combat .
This night , you will sleep in the giant 's castle .
And now you shall give me a piece of tobacco . ' '
I will .
But , I pray you , stay in the castle with me . '
` That I may not do , but on the morrow I will come . '
And on the morrow he do , and he bid Ian go to the giant 's stable where stand a horse to whom it matter nothing if she journey over land or sea .
` But be careful , ' he add , ` how you enter the stable , for the door swing without cease to and fro , and if it touch you , it will cause you to cry out .
I will go first and show you the way . '
` Go , ' say Ian .
And the raven give a bob and a hop , and think he be quite safe , but the door slam on a feather of his tail , and he scream loudly .
Then Ian take a run backwards , and a run forward , and make a spring ; but the door catch one of his foot , and he fell faint on the stable floor .
Quickly the raven pounce on him , and pick him up in his beak and claw , and carry him back to the castle , where he lay ointment on his foot till it be as well a ever it be .

And , first , give me a piece of tobacco . '
Many strange thing do Ian behold in that island , more than he have think for .
In a glen lay three hero stretch on their back , do to death by three spear that still stick in their breast .
But he keep his counsel and spake nothing , only he pull out the spear , and the men sit up and say : ` You be Ian the soldier 's son , and a spell be lay upon you to travel in our company , to the cave of the black fisherman . '
So together they go till they reach the cave , and one of the men enter , to see what should be find there .
And he behold a hag , horrible to look upon , seat on a rock , and before he could speak , she strike him with her club , and change him into a stone ; and in like manner she deal with the other three .
At the last Ian enter .
` These men be under spell , ' say the witch , ` and alive they can never be till you have anoint them with the water which you must fetch from the island of Big Women .
See that you do not tarry . '
And Ian turn away with a sinking heart , for he would fain have follow the young daughter of the knight of Grianaig .

But see that they touch her not , and give her food yourself , and yourself lead her into the stable , and shut the door .
And be sure that for every turn of the lock give by the sixteen stable lad you give one .
And now you shall break me off a piece of tobacco . '
The next morning Ian arose , and lead the horse from the stable , without the door hurt him , and he ride across the sea to the island of the Big Women , where the sixteen stable lad meet him , and each one offer to take his horse , and to fee her , and to put her into the stable .
But Ian only answer : ' I myself will put her in and will see to her . '
And thus he do .
And while he be rub her side the horse say to him : ` Every kind of drink will they offer you , but see you take none , save whey and water only . '
And so it fell out ; and when the sixteen stable-boys saw that he would drink nothing , they drink it all themselves , and one by one lay stretch around the board .
Then Ian felt pleased in his heart that he have withstand their fair word , and he forget the counsel that the horse have likewise give him say : ` Beware l you fall asleep , and let slip the chance of get home again ' ; for while the lad be sleep sweet music reach his ear , and he sleep also .
When this come to pass the steed break through the stable door , and kick him and wake him roughly .
` You do not heed my counsel , ' say she ; ` and who know if it be not too late to win over the sea ?
But first take that sword which hang on the wall , and cut off the head of the sixteen groom . '
Filled with shame at be once more proved heedless , Ian arise and do a the horse bid him .
Then he run to the well and pour some of the water into a leather bottle , and jump on the horse 's back rode over the sea to the island where the raven be wait for him .
` Lead the horse into the stable , ' say the raven , ` and lie down yourself to sleep , for to-morrow you must make the hero to live again , and must slay the hag .
And have a care not to be so foolish to-morrow a you be to-day . '
` Stay with me for company , ' beg Ian ; but the raven shake his head , and fly away .
In the morning Ian awoke , and hasten to the cave where the old hag be sit , and he strike her dead a she be , before she could cast spell on him .
Next he sprinkle the water over the hero , who come to life again , and together they all journey to the other side of the island , and there the raven meet them .
` At last you have follow the counsel that be give you , ' say the raven ; ` and now , have learn wisdom , you may go home again to Grianaig .
There you will find that the knight 's two eldest daughter be to be wed this day to your two brother , and the young to the chief of the men at the rock .
But her gold cap you shall give to me and , if you want it , you have only to think of me and I will bring it to you .
And one more warning I give you .
If anyone ask you whence you come , answer that you have come from behind you ; and if anyone ask you whither you be go , say that you be go before you . '
So Ian mount the horse and set her face to the sea and her back to the shore , and she be off , away and away till she reach the church of Grianaig , and there , in a field of grass , beside a well of water , he leap down from his saddle .
` Now , ' the horse say to him , ` draw your sword and cut off my head . '
But Ian answer : ` Poor thanks would that be for all the help I have have from you . '
` It be the only way that I can free myself from the spell that be lay by the giant on me and the raven ; for I be a girl and he be a youth woo me !
So have no fear , but do a I have say . '
Then Ian draw his sword a she bid him , and cut off her head , and go on his way without look backwards .
As he walk he saw a woman stand at her house door .
She ask him whence he have come , and he answer a the raven have tell him , that he come from behind .
Next she inquire whither he be go , and this time he make reply that he be go on before him , but that he be thirsty and would like a drink .
` You be an impudent fellow , ' say the woman ; ` but you shall have a drink . '
And she give him some milk , which be all she have till her husband come home .
` Where be your husband ? '
ask Ian , and the woman answer him : ` He be at the knight 's castle try to fashion gold and silver into a cap for the young daughter , like unto the cap that her sister wear , such a be not to be find in all this land .
But , see , he be return ; and now we shall hear how he have speed . '
At that the man enter the gate , and behold a strange youth , he say to him : ` What be your trade , boy ? ' '
I be a smith , ' reply Ian .
And the man answer : ` Good luck have befall me , then , for you can help me to make a cap for the knight 's daughter . '
` You can not make that cap , and you know it , ' say Ian .
` Well , I must try , ' reply the man , ` or I shall be hang on a tree ; so it be a good deed to help me . ' '
I will help you if I can , ' say Ian ; ` but keep the gold and silver for yourself , and lock me into the smithy to-night , and I will work my spell . '
So the man , wonder to himself , lock him in .
As soon a the key be turn in the lock Ian wish for the raven , and the raven come to him , carry the cap in his mouth .
` Now take my head off , ' say the raven .
But Ian answer : ` Poor thanks be that for all the help you have give me . '
` It be the only thanks you can give me , ' say the raven , ` for I be a youth like yourself before spell be lay on me . '
Then Ian draw his sword and cut off the head of the raven , and shut his eye so that he might see nothing .
After that he lay down and sleep till morning dawn , and the man come and unlock the door and shake the sleeper .
` Here be the cap , ' say Ian drowsily , draw it from under his pillow .
And he fell asleep again directly .
The sun be high in the heaven when he wake again , and this time he behold a tall , brown-haired youth standing by him . '
I be the raven , ' say the youth , ` and the spell be break .
But now get up and come with me . '
Then they two go together to the place where Ian have leave the dead horse ; but no horse be there now , only a beautiful maiden . '
I be the horse , ' she say , ` and the spell be break ' ; and she and the youth go away together .
In the meantime the smith have carry the cap to the castle , and bid a servant belonging to the knight 's young daughter bear it to her mistress .
But when the girl 's eye fell on it , she cry out : ` He speak false ; and if he do not bring me the man who really make the cap I will hang him on the tree beside my window . '
The servant be fill with fear at her word , and hasten and tell the smith , who run as fast a he could to seek for Ian .
And when he find him and bring him into the castle , the girl be first struck dumb with joy ; then she declare that she would marry nobody else .
At this some one fetch to her the knight of Grianaig , and when Ian have tell his tale , he vow that the maiden be right , and that his elder daughter should never wed with men who have not only take glory to themselves which do not belong to them , but have leave the real doer of the deed to his fate .
And the wedding guest say that the knight have speak well ; and the two elder brother be fain to leave the country , for no one would converse with them .
The Fox and the Wolf -LSB- From Tales of the West Highlands . -RSB-
At the foot of some high mountain there be , once upon a time , a small village , and a little way off two road meet , one of them go to the east and the other to the west .
The villager be quiet , hard-working folk , who toil in the field all day , and in the evening set out for home when the bell begin to ring in the little church .
In the summer morning they lead out their flock to pasture , and be happy and content from sunrise to sunset .
One summer night , when a round full moon shine down upon the white road , a great wolf come trot round the corner . '
I positively must get a good meal before I go back to my den , ' he say to himself ; ` it be nearly a week since I have taste anything but scrap , though perhaps no one would think it to look at my figure !
Of course there be plenty of rabbit and hare in the mountain ; but indeed one need to be a greyhound to catch them , and I be not so young a I be !
If I could only dine off that fox I saw a fortnight ago , curl up into a delicious hairy ball , I should ask nothing well ; I would have eat her then , but unluckily her husband be lie beside her , and one know that fox , great and small , run like the wind .
Really it seem a if there be not a living creature leave for me to prey upon but a wolf , and , a the proverb say : `` One wolf do not bite another . ''
However , let u see what this village can produce .
I be as hungry a a schoolmaster . '
Now , while these thought be run through the mind of the wolf , the very fox he have be think of be gallop along the other road .
` The whole of this day I have listen to those village hens cluck till I could bear it no longer , ' murmur she a she bound along , hardly seem to touch the ground .
` When you be fond of fowl and egg it be the sweet of all music .
As sure a there be a sun in heaven I will have some of them this night , for I have grow so thin that my very bones rattle , and my poor baby be cry for food . '
And a she speak she reach a little plot of grass , where the two road join , and fling herself under a tree to take a little rest , and to settle her plan .
At this moment the wolf come up .
At the sight of the fox lie within his grasp his mouth begin to water , but his joy be somewhat checked when he notice how thin she be .
The fox 's quick ear hear the sound of his paw , though they be soft a velvet , and turn her head she say politely : ` Is that you , neighbour ?
What a strange place to meet in !
I hope you be quite well ? '
` Quite well a regard my health , ' answer the wolf , whose eye glisten greedily , ` at least , as well a one can be when one be very hungry .
But what be the matter with you ?
A fortnight ago you be as plump a heart could wish ! ' '
I have be ill -- very ill , ' reply the fox , ` and what you say be quite true .
A worm be fat in comparison with me . '
` He be .
Still , you be good enough for me ; for `` to the hungry no bread be hard . '' '
` Oh , you be always joke !
I 'm sure you be not half as hungry a I ! '
` That we shall soon see , ' cry the wolf , open his huge mouth and crouching for a spring .
` What be you do ? '
exclaim the fox , step backwards .
` What be I do ?
What I be go to do be to make my supper off you , in less time than a cock take to crow . '
` Well , I suppose you must have your joke , ' answer the fox lightly , but never remove her eye from the wolf , who reply with a snarl which show all his teeth : ' I do n't want to joke , but to eat ! '
` But surely a person of your talent must perceive that you might eat me to the very last morsel and never know that you have swallow anything at all ! '
` In this world the clever people be always the hungry , ' reply the wolf .
` Ah !
how true that be ; but -- ' ' I ca n't stop to listen to your `` buts '' and `` yets , '' ' broke in the wolf rudely ; ` let u get to the point , and the point be that I want to eat you and not talk to you . '
` Have you no pity for a poor mother ? '
ask the fox , put her tail to her eye , but peep slily out of them all the same . '
I be die of hunger , ' answer the wolf , doggedly ; ` and you know , ' he add with a grin , ` that charity begin at home . '
` Quite so , ' reply the fox ; ` it would be unreasonable of me to object to your satisfy your appetite at my expense .
But if the fox resign herself to the sacrifice , the mother offer you one last request . '
` Then be quick and do n't waste my time , for I ca n't wait much longer .
What be it you want ? '
` You must know , ' say the fox , ` that in this village there be a rich man who make in the summer enough cheese to last him for the whole year , and keep them in an old well , now dry , in his courtyard .
By the well hang two bucket on a pole that be use , in former day , to draw up water .
For many night I have creep down to the palace , and have lower myself in the bucket , bring home with me enough cheese to fee the child .
All I beg of you be to come with me , and , instead of hunt chicken and such thing , I will make a good meal off cheese before I die . '
` But the cheese may be all finish by now ? '
` If you be only to see the quantity of them ! '
laugh the fox .
` And even if they be finish , there would always be ME to eat . '
` Well , I will come .
Lead the way , but I warn you that if you try to escape or play any trick you be reckon without your host -- that be to say , without my leg , which be as long a yours ! '
All be silent in the village , and not a light be to be see but that of the moon , which shine bright and clear in the sky .
The wolf and the fox creep softly along , when suddenly they stop and look at each other ; a savoury smell of fry bacon reach their nose , and reach the nose of the sleep dog , who begin to bark greedily .
` Is it safe to go on , think you ? '
ask the wolf in a whisper .
And the fox shake her head .
` Not while the dog be bark , ' say she ; ` someone might come out to see if anything be the matter . '
And she sign to the wolf to curl himself up in the shadow beside her .
In about half an hour the dog grow tire of barking , or perhaps the bacon be eat up and there be no smell to excite them .
Then the wolf and the fox jump up , and hasten to the foot of the wall . '
I be lighter than he be , ' think the fox to herself , ` and perhaps if I make haste I can get a start , and jump over the wall on the other side before he manage to spring over this one . '
And she quicken her pace .
But if the wolf could not run he could jump , and with one bound he be beside his companion .
` What be you go to do , comrade ? '
` Oh , nothing , ' reply the fox , much vexed at the failure of her plan . '
I think if I be to take a bit out of your haunch you would jump well , ' say the wolf , give a snap at her a he speak .
The fox draw back uneasily .
` Be careful , or I shall scream , ' she snarl .
And the wolf , understand all that might happen if the fox carry out her threat , give a signal to his companion to leap on the wall , where he immediately follow her .
Once on the top they crouch down and look about them .
Not a creature be to be see in the courtyard , and in the furthest corner from the house stand the well , with it two bucket suspend from a pole , just a the fox have describe it .
The two thief drag themselves noiselessly along the wall till they be opposite the well , and by stretch out her neck as far a it would go the fox be able to make out that there be only very little water in the bottom , but just enough to reflect the moon , big , and round and yellow .
` How lucky ! '
cry she to the wolf .
` There be a huge cheese about the size of a mill wheel .
Look !
look !
do you ever see anything so beautiful ! '
` Never ! '
answer the wolf , peer over in his turn , his eye glisten greedily , for he imagine that the moon 's reflection in the water be really a cheese .
` And now , unbeliever , what have you to say ? '
and the fox laugh gently .
` That you be a woman -- I mean a fox -- of your word , ' reply the wolf .
` Well , then , go down in that bucket and eat your fill , ' say the fox .
` Oh , be that your game ? '
ask the wolf , with a grin .
` No !
no !
The person who go down in the bucket will be you !
And if you do n't go down your head will go without you ! '
` Of course I will go down , with the great pleasure , ' answer the fox , who have expect the wolf 's reply .
` And be sure you do n't eat all the cheese , or it will be the bad for you , ' continue the wolf .
But the fox look up at him with tear in her eye .
` Farewell , suspicious one ! '
she say sadly .
And climb into the bucket .
In an instant she have reach the bottom of the well , and find that the water be not deep enough to cover her leg .
` Why , it be large and rich than I think , ' cry she , turn towards the wolf , who be lean over the wall of the well .
` Then be quick and bring it up , ' command the wolf .
` How can I , when it weigh more than I do ? '
ask the fox .
` If it be so heavy bring it in two bit , of course , ' say he .
` But I have no knife , ' answer the fox .
` You will have to come down yourself , and we will carry it up between u . '
` And how be I to come down ? '
inquire the wolf .
` Oh , you be really very stupid !
Get into the other bucket that be nearly over your head . '
The wolf look up , and saw the bucket hang there , and with some difficulty he climb into it .
As he weigh at least four time as much a the fox the bucket go down with a jerk , and the other bucket , in which the fox be seat , come to the surface .
As soon a he understand what be happen , the wolf begin to speak like an angry wolf , but be a little comforted when he remember that the cheese still remain to him .
` But where be the cheese ? '
he ask of the fox , who in her turn be lean over the parapet watch his proceeding with a smile .
` The cheese ? '
answer the fox ; ` why I be take it home to my baby , who be too young to get food for themselves . '
` Ah , traitor ! '
cry the wolf , howl with rage .
But the fox be not there to hear this insult , for she have go off to a neighbouring fowl-house , where she have notice some fat young chicken the day before .
` Perhaps I do treat him rather badly , ' she say to herself .
` But it seem get cloudy , and if there should be heavy rain the other bucket will fill and sink to the bottom , and his will go up -- at least it may ! '
How Ian Direach Got the Blue Falcon -LSB- From Cuentos Populares , por Antonio de Trueba . -RSB-
Long ago a king and queen rule over the island of the west , and they have one son , whom they love dearly .
The boy grow up to be tall and strong and handsome , and he could run and shoot , and swim and dive well than any lad of his own age in the country .
Besides , he know how to sail about , and sing song to the harp , and during the winter evening , when everyone be gather round the huge hall fire shape bow or weave cloth , Ian Direach would tell them tale of the deed of his father .
So the time slip by till Ian be almost a man , a they reckon men in those day , and then his mother the queen die .
There be great mourn throughout all the isle , and the boy and his father mourn her bitterly also ; but before the new year come the king have marry another wife , and seem to have forget his old one .
Only Ian remember .
On a morning when the leaf be yellow in the tree of the glen , Ian sling his bow over his shoulder , and fill his quiver with arrow , go on to the hill in search of game .
But not a bird be to be see anywhere , till at length a blue falcon fly past him , and raise his bow he take aim at her .
His eye be straight and his hand steady , but the falcon 's flight be swift , and he only shoot a feather from her wing .
As the sun be now low over the sea he put the feather in his game bag , and set out homewards .
` Have you bring me much game to-day ? '
ask his stepmother a he enter the hall .
` Nought save this , ' he answer , hand her the feather of the blue falcon , which she hold by the tip and gaze at silently .
Then she turn to Ian and say : ' I be set it on you a cross and a spell , and a the fall of the year !
That you may always be cold , and wet and dirty , and that your shoe may ever have pool in them , till you bring me hither the blue falcon on which that feather grow . '
` If it be spell you be lay I can lay them too , ' answered Ian Direach ; ` and you shall stand with one foot on the great house and another on the castle , till I come back again , and your face shall be to the wind , from wheresoever it shall blow . '
Then he go away to seek the bird , a his stepmother bid him ; and , look homewards from the hill , he saw the queen stand with one foot on the great house , and the other on the castle , and her face turn towards whatever tempest should blow .
On he journey , over hill , and through river till he reach a wide plain , and never a glimpse do he catch of the falcon .
Darker and darker it grow , and the small bird be seek their nest , and at length Ian Direach could see no more , and he lay down under some bush and sleep come to him .
And in his dream a soft nose touch him , and a warm body curl up beside him , and a low voice whisper to him : ` Fortune be against you , Ian Direach ; I have but the cheek and the hoof of a sheep to give you , and with these you must be content . '
With that Ian Direach awake , and behold Gille Mairtean the fox .
Between them they kindle a fire , and eat their supper .
Then Gille Mairtean the fox bade Ian Direach lie down a before , and sleep till morning .
And in the morning , when he awake , Gille Mairtean say : ` The falcon that you seek be in the keep of the Giant of the Five Heads , and the Five Necks , and the Five Humps .
I will show you the way to his house , and I counsel you to do his bidding , nimbly and cheerfully , and , above all , to treat his bird kindly , for in this manner he may give you his falcon to fee and care for .
And when this happen , wait till the giant be out of his house ; then throw a cloth over the falcon and bear her away with you .
Only see that not one of her feather touch anything within the house , or evil will befall you . ' '
I thank you for your counsel , ' spake Ian Direach , ` and I will be careful to follow it . '
Then he take the path to the giant 's house .
` Who be there ? '
cry the giant , a someone knock loudly on the door of his house .
` One who seek work a a servant , ' answered Ian Direach .
` And what can you do ? '
ask the giant again . '
I can fee bird and tend pig ; I can fee and milk a cow , and also goat and sheep , if you have any of these , ' reply Ian Direach .
` Then enter , for I have great need of such a one , ' say the giant .
So Ian Direach enter , and tend so well and carefully all the bird and beast , that the giant be well satisfy than ever he have be , and at length he think that he might even be trust to fee the falcon .
And the heart of Ian be glad , and he tend the blue falcon till his father shine like the sky , and the giant be well pleased ; and one day he say to him : ` For long my brother on the other side of the mountain have beseech me to visit them , but never could I go for fear of my falcon .
Now I think I can leave her with you for one day , and before nightfall I shall be back again . '
Scarcely be the giant out of sight next morning when Ian Direach seize the falcon , and throw a cloth over her head hasten with her to the door .
