But the ray of the sun pierce through the thickness of the cloth , and a they pass the doorpost she give a spring , and the tip of one of her feather touch the post , which give a scream , and bring the giant back in three stride .
Ian Direach tremble a he saw him ; but the giant only say : ` If you wish for my falcon you must first bring me the White Sword of Light that be in the house of the Big Women of Dhiurradh . '
` And where do they live ? '
ask Ian .
But the giant answer : ` Ah , that be for you to discover . '
And Ian dare say no more , and hasten down to the waste .
There , a he hop , he meet his friend Gille Mairtean the fox , who bid him eat his supper and lie down to sleep .
And when he have waken next morning the fox say to him : ` Let u go down to the shore of the sea . '
And to the shore of the sea they go .
And after they have reach the shore , and behold the sea stretch before them , and the isle of Dhiurradh in the midst of it , the soul of Ian sank , and he turn to Gille Mairtean and ask why he have bring him thither , for the giant , when he have send him , have know full well that without a boat he could never find the Big Women .
` Do not be cast down , ' answer the fox , ` it be quite easy !
I will change myself into a boat , and you shall go on board me , and I will carry you over the sea to the Seven Big Women of Dhiurradh .
Tell them that you be skilled in brighten silver and gold , and in the end they will take you a servant , and if you be careful to please them they will give you the White Sword of Light to make bright and shining .
But when you seek to steal it , take heed that it sheath touch nothing inside the house , or ill will befall you . '
So Ian Direach do all thing a the fox have tell him , and the Seven Big Women of Dhiurradh take him for their servant , and for six week he work so hard that his seven mistress say to each other : ` Never have a servant have the skill to make all bright and shin like this one .
Let u give him the White Sword of Light to polish like the rest . '
Then they bring forth the White Sword of Light from the iron closet where it hang , and bid him rub it till he could see his face in the shining blade ; and he do so .
But one day , when the Seven Big Women be out of the way , he bethink him that the moment have come for him to carry off the sword , and , replace it in it sheath , he hoist it on his shoulder .
But just a he be pass through the door the tip of the sheath touch it , and the door give a loud shriek .
And the Big Women hear it , and come run back , and take the sword from him , and say : ` If it be our sword you want , you must first bring u the bay colt of the King of Erin . '
Humbled and ashamed , Ian Direach leave the house , and sat by the side of the sea , and soon Gille Mairtean the fox come to him .
` Plainly I see that you have take no heed to my word , Ian Direach , ' speak the fox .
` But eat first , and yet once more will I help you . '
At these word the heart return again to Ian Direach , and he gather stick and make a fire and ate with Gille Mairtean the fox , and sleep on the sand .
At dawn next morning Gille Mairtean say to Ian Direach : ' I will change myself into a ship , and will bear you across the sea to Erin , to the land where dwell the king .
And you shall offer yourself to serve in his stable , and to tend his horse , till at length so well content be he , that he give you the bay colt to wash and brush .
But when you run away with her see that nought except the sol of her hoof touch anything within the palace gate , or it will go ill with you . '
After he have thus counsel Ian Direach , the fox change himself into a ship , and set sail for Erin .
And the king of that country give into Ian Direach 's hand the care of his horse , and never before do their skin shine so brightly or be their pace so swift .
And the king be well pleased , and at the end of a month he send for Ian and say to him : ` You have give me faithful service , and now I will entrust you with the most precious thing that my kingdom hold . '
And when he have speak , he lead Ian Direach to the stable where stand the bay colt .
And Ian rub her and feed her , and gallop with her all round the country , till he could leave one wind behind him and catch the other which be in front . '
I be go away to hunt , ' say the king one morning while he be watch Ian tend the bay colt in her stable .
` The deer have come down from the hill , and it be time for me to give them chase . '
Then he go away ; and when he be no longer in sight , Ian Direach lead the bay colt out of the stable , and sprang on her back .
But a they ride through the gate , which stand between the palace and the outer world , the colt swish her tail against the post , which shriek loudly .
In a moment the king come run up , and he seize the colt 's bridle .
` If you want my bay colt , you must first bring me the daughter of the king of the Franks . '
With slow step go Ian Direach down to the shore where Gille Mairtean the fox await him .
` Plainly I see that you have not do a I bid you , nor will you ever do it , ' speak Gille Mairtean the fox ; ` but I will help you yet again for a third time I will change myself into a ship , and we will sail to France . '
And to France they sail , and , a he be the ship , the Gille Mairtean sail where he would , and run himself into the cleft of a rock , high on to the land .
Then , he command Ian Direach to go up to the king 's palace , say that he have be wreck , that his ship be make fast in a rock , and that none have be save but himself only .
Ian Direach listen to the word of the fox , and he tell a tale so pitiful , that the king and queen , and the princess their daughter , all come out to hear it .
And when they have hear , nought would please them except to go down to the shore and visit the ship , which by now be float , for the tide be up .
Torn and batter be she , a if she have pass through many danger , yet music of a wondrous sweetness pour forth from within .
` Bring hither a boat , ' cry the princess , ` that I may go and see for myself the harp that give forth such music . '
And a boat be bring , and Ian Direach step in to row it to the side of the ship .
To the further side he row , so that none could see , and when he help the princess on board he give a push to the boat , so that she could not get back to it again .
And the music sound always sweeter , though they could never see whence it come , and seek it from one part of the vessel to another .
When at last they reach the deck and look around them , nought of land could they see , or anything save the rushing water .
The princess stand silent , and her face grow grim .
At last she say : ` An ill trick have you play me !
What be this that you have do , and whither be we go ? '
` It be a queen you will be , ' answered Ian Direach , ` for the king of Erin have send me for you , and in return he will give me his bay colt , that I may take him to the Seven Big Women of Dhiurradh , in exchange for the White Sword of Light .
This I must carry to the giant of the Five Heads and Five Necks and Five Humps , and , in place of it , he will bestow on me the blue falcon , which I have promise my stepmother , so that she may free me from the spell which she have lay on me . ' '
I would rather be wife to you , ' answer the princess .
By-and-by the ship sail into a harbour on the coast of Erin , and cast anchor there .
And Gille Mairtean the fox bade Ian Direach tell the princess that she must bide yet a while in a cave amongst the rock , for they have business on land , and after a while they would return to her .
Then they take a boat and row up to some rock , and a they touch the land Gille Mairtean change himself into a fair woman , who laugh , and say to Ian Direach , ' I will give the king a fine wife . '
Now the king of Erin have be hunt on the hill , and when he saw a strange ship sailing towards the harbour , he guess that it might be Ian Direach , and leave his hunting , and run down to the hill to the stable .
Hastily he lead the bay colt from his stall , and put the golden saddle on her back , and the silver bridle over his head , and with the colt 's bridle in his hand , he hurry to meet the princess . '
I have bring you the king of France 's daughter , ' say Ian Direach .
And the king of Erin look at the maiden , and be well pleased , not know that it be Gille Mairtean the fox .
And he bow low , and beseech her to do him the honour to enter the palace ; and Gille Mairtean , a he go in , turn to look back at Ian Direach , and laugh .
In the great hall the king pause and point to an iron chest which stand in a corner .
` In that chest be the crown that have wait for you for many year , ' he say , ` and at last you have come for it . '
And he stoop down to unlock the box .
In an instant Gille Mairtean the fox have spring on his back , and give him such a bite that he fell down unconscious .
Quickly the fox take his own shape again , and gallop away to the sea shore , where Ian Direach and the princess and the bay colt await him . '
I will become a ship , ' cry Gille Mairtean , ` and you shall go on board me . '
And so he do , and Ian Direach let the bay colt into the ship and the princess go after them , and they set sail for Dhiurradh .
The wind be behind them , and very soon they saw the rock of Dhiurradh in front .
Then speak Gille Mairtean the fox : ` Let the bay colt and the king 's daughter hide in these rock , and I will change myself into the colt , and go with you to the house of the Seven Big Women . '
Joy file the heart of the Big Women when they behold the bay colt lead up to their door by Ian Direach .
And the young of them fetch the White Sword of Light , and give it into the hand of Ian Direach , who take off the golden saddle and the silver bridle , and go down the hill with the sword to the place where the princess and the real colt await him .
` Now we shall have the ride that we have long for ! '
cry the Seven Big Women ; and they saddle and bridle the colt , and the eldest one get upon the saddle .
Then the second sister sit on the back of the first , and the third on the back of the second , and so on for the whole seven .
And when they be all seat , the eldest strike her side with a whip and the colt bound forward .
Over the moor she fly , and round and round the mountain , and still the Big Women clung to her and snort with pleasure .
At last she leap high in the air , and come down on top of Monadh the high hill , where the crag be .
And she rest her fore foot on the crag , and throw up her hind leg , and the Seven Big Women fell over the crag , and be dead when they reach the bottom .
And the colt laugh , and become a fox again and gallop away to the sea shore , where Ian Direach , and the princess and the real colt and the White Sword of Light be await him . '
I will make myself into a ship , ' say Gille Mairtean the fox , ` and will carry you and the princess , and the bay colt and the White Sword of Light , back to the land . '
And when the shore be reach , Gille Mairtean the fox take back his own shape , and speak to Ian Direach in this wise : ` Let the princess and the White Sword of Light , and the bay colt , remain among the rock , and I will change myself into the likeness of the White Sword of Light , and you shall bear me to the giant , and , instead , he will give you the blue falcon . '
And Ian Direach do a the fox bid him , and set out for the giant 's castle .
From afar the giant behold the blaze of the White Sword of Light , and his heart rejoice ; and he take the blue falcon and put it in a basket , and give it to Ian Direach , who bore it swiftly away to the place where the princess , and the bay colt , and the real Sword of Light be await him .
So well content be the giant to possess the sword he have covet for many a year , that he begin at once to whirl it through the air , and to cut and slash with it .
For a little while Gille Mairtean let the giant play with him in this manner ; then he turn in the giant 's hand , and cut through the Five Necks , so that the Five Heads roll on the ground .
Afterwards he go back to Ian Direach and say to him : ` Saddle the colt with the golden saddle , and bridle her with the silver bridle , and sling the basket with the falcon over your shoulder , and hold the White Sword of Light with it back against your nose .
Then mount the colt , and let the princess mount behind you , and ride thus to your father 's palace .
But see that the back of the sword be ever against your nose , else when your stepmother beholds you , she will change you into a dry faggot .
If , however , you do a I bid you , she will become herself a bundle of stick . '
Ian Direach hearken to the word of Gille Mairtean , and his stepmother fell a a bundle of stick before him ; and he set fire to her , and be free from her spell for ever .
After that he marry the princess , who be the best wife in all the island of the West .
Henceforth he be safe from harm , for have he not the bay colt who could leave one wind behind her and catch the other wind , and the blue falcon to bring him game to eat , and the White Sword of Light to pierce through his foe ?
And Ian Direach know that all this he owe to Gille Mairtean the fox , and he make a compact with him that he might choose any beast out of his herd , whenever hunger seize him , and that henceforth no arrow should be let fly at him or at any of his race .
But Gille Mairtean the fox would take no reward for the help he have give to Ian Direach , only his friendship .
Thus all thing prosper with Ian Direach till he die .
The Ugly Duckling -LSB- From Tales of the West Highlands . -RSB-
It be summer in the land of Denmark , and though for most of the year the country look flat and ugly , it be beautiful now .
The wheat be yellow , the oat be green , the hay be dry and delicious to roll in , and from the old ruin house which nobody live in , down to the edge of the canal , be a forest of great burdock , so tall that a whole family of child might have dwell in them and never have be find out .
It be under these burdock that a duck have build herself a warm nest , and be not sit all day on six pretty egg .
Five of them be white , but the sixth , which be large than the others , be of an ugly grey colour .
The duck be always puzzle about that egg , and how it come to be so different from the rest .
Other bird might have think that when the duck go down in the morning and evening to the water to stretch her leg in a good swim , some lazy mother might have be on the watch , and have pop her egg into the nest .
But duck be not clever at all , and be not quick at counting , so this duck do not worry herself about the matter , but just take care that the big egg should be as warm a the rest .
This be the first set of egg that the duck have ever lay , and , to begin with , she be very pleased and proud , and laugh at the other mother , who be always neglect their duty to gossip with each other or to take little extra swim besides the two in the morning and evening that be necessary for health .
But at length she grow tired of sit there all day .
` Surely eggs take longer hatch than they do , ' she say to herself ; and she pin for a little amusement also .
Still , she know that if she leave her egg and the duckling in them to die none of her friend would ever speak to her again ; so there she stay , only get off the eggs several time a day to see if the shell be crack -- which may have be the very reason why they do not crack sooner .
She have look at the egg at least a hundred and fifty time , when , to her joy , she saw a tiny crack on two of them , and scramble back to the nest she draw the egg closer the one to the other , and never move for the whole of that day .
Next morning she be reward by notice crack in the whole five egg , and by midday two little yellow head be poke out from the shell .
This encourage her so much that , after break the shell with her bill , so that the little creature could get free of them , she sit steadily for a whole night upon the nest , and before the sun arise the five white egg be empty , and ten pair of eye be gaze out upon the green world .
Now the duck have be carefully bring up , and do not like dirt , and , besides , broken shell be not at all comfortable thing to sit or walk upon ; so she push the rest out over the side , and felt delight to have some company to talk to till the big egg hatch .
But day after day go on , and the big egg show no sign of cracking , and the duck grow more and more impatient , and begin to wish to consult her husband , who never come . '
I ca n't think what be the matter with it , ' the duck grumble to her neighbour who have call in to pay her a visit .
` Why I could have hatch two brood in the time that this one have take ! '
` Let me look at it , ' say the old neighbour .
` Ah , I think so ; it be a turkey 's egg .
Once , when I be young , they trick me to sit on a brood of turkey 's egg myself , and when they be hatch the creature be so stupid that nothing would make them learn to swim .
I have no patience when I think of it . '
` Well , I will give it another chance , ' sigh the duck , ` and if it do not come out of it shell in another twenty-four hour , I will just leave it alone and teach the rest of them to swim properly and to find their own food .
I really ca n't be expect to do two thing at once . '
And with a fluff of her feather she push the egg into the middle of the nest .
All through the next day she sit on , give up even her morning bath for fear that a blast of cold might strike the big egg .
In the evening , when she venture to peep , she think she saw a tiny crack in the upper part of the shell .
Filled with hope , she go back to her duty , though she could hardly sleep all night for excitement .
When she wake with the first steak of light she felt something stir under her .
Yes , there it be at last ; and a she move , a big awkward bird tumble head foremost on the ground .
There be no deny it be ugly , even the mother be force to admit that to herself , though she only say it be ` large ' and ` strong . '
` You wo n't need any teaching when you be once in the water , ' she tell him , with a glance of surprise at the dull brown which cover his back , and at his long naked neck .
And indeed he do not , though he be not half so pretty to look at a the little yellow ball that follow her .
When they return they find the old neighbour on the bank wait for them to take them into the duckyard .
` No , it be not a young turkey , certainly , ' whisper she in confidence to the mother , ` for though it be lean and skinny , and have no colour to speak of , yet there be something rather distinguish about it , and it hold it head up well . '
` It be very kind of you to say so , ' answer the mother , who by this time have some secret doubt of it loveliness .
` Of course , when you see it by itself it be all right , though it be different , somehow , from the others .
But one can not expect all one 's child to be beautiful ! '
By this time they have reach the centre of the yard , where a very old duck be sit , who be treat with great respect by all the fowls present .
` You must go up and bow low before her , ' whisper the mother to her child , nod her head in the direction of the old lady , ` and keep your leg well apart , a you see me do .
No well-bred duckling turn in it toe .
It be a sign of common parent . '
The little duck try hard to make their small fat body copy the movement of their mother , and the old lady be quite pleased with them ; but the rest of the duck look on discontentedly , and say to each other : ` Oh , dear me , here be ever so many more !
The yard be full already ; and do you ever see anything quite as ugly a that great tall creature ?
He be a disgrace to any brood .
I shall go and chase him out ! '
So say she put up her feather , and run to the big duckling bit his neck .
The duckling give a loud quack ; it be the first time he have felt any pain , and at the sound his mother turn quickly .
` Leave him alone , ' she say fiercely , ` or I will send for his father .
He be not trouble you . '
` No ; but he be so ugly and awkward no one can put up with him , ' answer the stranger .
And though the duckling do not understand the meaning of the word , he felt he be be blame , and become more uncomfortable still when the old Spanish duck who rule the fowlyard struck in : ` It certainly be a great pity he be so different from these beautiful darling .
If he could only be hatch over again ! '
The poor little fellow droop his head , and do not know where to look , but be comfort when his mother answer : ` He may not be quite as handsome a the others , but he swim good , and be very strong ; I be sure he will make his way in the world as well a anybody . '
` Well , you must feel quite at home here , ' say the old duck waddle off .
And so they do , all except the duckling , who be snap at by everyone when they think his mother be not look .
Even the turkey-cock , who be so big , never pass him without mock word , and his brother and sister , who would not have notice any difference unless it have be put into their head , soon become a rude and unkind a the rest .
At last he could bear it no longer , and one day he fancy he saw sign of his mother turn against him too ; so that night , when the duck and hen be still asleep , he steal away through an open door , and under cover of the burdock leave scramble on by the bank of the canal , till he reach a wide grassy moor , full of soft marshy place where the reed grow .
Here he lay down , but he be too tired and too frightened to fall asleep , and with the early peep of the sun the reed begin to rustle , and he saw that he have blunder into a colony of wild duck .
But a he could not run away again he stand up and bow politely .
` You be ugly , ' say the wild duck , when they have look him well over ; ` but , however , it be no business of ours , unless you wish to marry one of our daughter , and that we should not allow . '
And the duckling answer that he have no idea of marry anybody , and want nothing but to be leave alone after his long journey .
So for two whole day he lay quietly among the reed , eat such food a he could find , and drink the water of the moorland pool , till he felt himself quite strong again .
He wish he might stay be he be for ever , he be so comfortable and happy , away from everyone , with nobody to bite him and tell him how ugly he be .
He be think these thought , when two young gander catch sight of him a they be have their evening splash among the reed , look for their supper .
` We be get tire of this moor , ' they say , ` and to-morrow we think of try another , where the lake be large and the feeding well .
Will you come with u ? '
` Is it nicer than this ? '
ask the duckling doubtfully .
And the word be hardly out of his mouth , when ` Pif !
pah ! '
and the two new-comers be stretch dead beside him .
At the sound of the gun the wild duck in the rush fly into the air , and for a few minute the fire continue .
Luckily for himself the duckling could not fly , and he flounder along through the water till he could hide himself amidst some tall fern which grow in a hollow .
But before he get there he meet a huge creature on four leg , which he afterwards know to be a dog , who stand and gaze at him with a long red tongue hang out of his mouth .
The duckling grow cold with terror , and try to hide his head beneath his little wing ; but the dog snuff at him and pass on , and he be able to reach his place of shelter . '
I be too ugly even for a dog to eat , ' say he to himself .
` Well , that be a great mercy . '
And he curl himself up in the soft grass till the shot die away in the distance .
When all have be quiet for a long time , and there be only star to see him , he creep out and look about him .
He would never go near a pool again , never , think he ; and see that the moor stretch far away in the opposite direction from which he have come , he march bravely on till he get to a small cottage , which seem too tumbledown for the stone to hold together many hour longer .
Even the door only hang upon one hinge , and a the only light in the room sprang from a tiny fire , the duckling edge himself cautiously in , and lay down under a chair close to the broken door , from which he could get out if necessary .
But no one seem to see him or smell him ; so he spend the rest of the night in peace .
Now in the cottage dwell an old woman , her cat , and a hen ; and it be really they , and not she , who be master of the house .
The old woman , who pass all her day in spin yarn , which she sell at the near town , love both the cat and the hen a her own child , and never contradict them in any way ; so it be their grace , and not hers , that the duckling would have to gain .
It be only next morning , when it grow light , that they notice their visitor , who stand tremble before them , with his eye on the door ready to escape at any moment .
They do not , however , appear very fierce , and the duckling become less afraid a they approach him .
` Can you lay egg ? '
ask the hen .
And the duckling answer meekly : ` No ; I do n't know how . '
Upon which the hen turn her back , and the cat come forward .
` Can you ruffle your fur when you be angry , or purr when you be please ? '
say she .
And again the duckling have to admit that he could do nothing but swim , which do not seem of much use to anybody .
So the cat and the hen go straight off to the old woman , who be still in bed .
` Such a useless creature have take refuge here , ' they say .
` It call itself a duckling ; but it can neither lay egg nor purr !
What have we well do with it ? '
` Keep it , to be sure ! '
reply the old woman briskly .
` It be all nonsense about it not lay egg .
Anyway , we will let it stay here for a bit , and see what happen . '
So the duckling remain for three week , and share the food of the cat and the hen ; but nothing in the way of egg happen at all .
Then the sun come out , and the air grow soft , and the duckling grow tired of be in a hut , and want with all his might to have a swim .
And one morning he get so restless that even his friend notice it .
` What be the matter ? '
ask the hen ; and the duckling tell her . '
I be so long for the water again .
You ca n't think how delicious it be to put your head under the water and dive straight to the bottom . ' '
I do n't think I should enjoy it , ' reply the hen doubtfully .
` And I do n't think the cat would like it either . '
And the cat , when ask , agree there be nothing she would hate so much . '
I ca n't stay here any long , I Must get to the water , ' repeat the duck .
And the cat and the hen , who felt hurt and offend , answer shortly : ` Very well then , go . '
The duckling would have like to say good-bye , and thank them for their kindness , a he be polite by nature ; but they have both turn their back on him , so he go out of the rickety door feel rather sad .
But , in spite of himself , he could not help a thrill of joy when he be out in the air and water once more , and care little for the rude glance of the creature he meet .
For a while he be quite happy and content ; but soon the winter come on , and snow begin to fall , and everything to grow very wet and uncomfortable .
And the duckling soon find that it be one thing to enjoy be in the water , and quite another to like be damp on land .
The sun be set one day , like a great scarlet globe , and the river , to the duckling 's vast bewilderment , be get hard and slippery , when he hear a sound of whir wing , and high up in the air a flock of swan be fly .
They be as white a snow which have fall during the night , and their long neck with yellow bill be stretch southward , for they be go -- they do not quite know whither -- but to a land where the sun shone all day .
Oh , if he only could have go with them !
But that be not possible , of course ; and besides , what sort of companion could an ugly thing like him be to those beautiful being ?
So he walk sadly down to a sheltered pool and dive to the very bottom , and try to think it be the great happiness he could dream of .
But , all the same , he know it be n't !
And every morning it grow colder and colder , and the duckling have hard work to keep himself warm .
Indeed , it would be truer to say that he never be warm at all ; and at last , after one bitter night , his leg move so slowly that the ice creep closer and closer , and when the morning light break he be catch fast , a in a trap ; and soon his sens go from him .
A few hour more and the poor duckling 's life have be end .
But , by good fortune , a man be cross the river on his way to his work , and saw in a moment what have happen .
He have on thick wooden shoe , and he go and stamp so hard on the ice that it break , and then he pick up the duckling and tuck him under his sheepskin coat , where his frozen bone begin to thaw a little .
Instead of go on his work , the man turn back and take the bird to his child , who give him a warm mess to eat and put him in a box by the fire , and when they come back from school he be much more comfortable than he have be since he have leave the old woman 's cottage .
They be kind little child , and want to play with him ; but , ala !
the poor fellow have never play in his life , and think they want to tease him , and fly straight into the milk-pan , and then into the butter-dish , and from that into the meal-barrel , and at last , terrify at the noise and confusion , right out of the door , and hid himself in the snow amongst the bush at the back of the house .
He never could tell afterwards exactly how he have spend the rest of the winter .
He only know that he be very miserable and that he never have enough to eat .
But by-and-by thing grow well .
The earth become soft , the sun hotter , the bird sang , and the flower once more appeared in the grass .
When he stand up , he felt different , somehow , from what he have do before he fell asleep among the reed to which he have wander after he have escape from the peasant 's hut .
His body seem large , and his wing strong .
Something pink look at him from the side of a hill .
He think he would fly towards it and see what it be .
Oh , how glorious it felt to be rush through the air , wheel first one way and then the other !
He have never think that fly could be like that !
The duckling be almost sorry when he draw near the pink cloud and find it be make up of apple blossom grow beside a cottage whose garden run down to the bank of the canal .
He flutter slowly to the ground and pause for a few minute under a thicket of syringa , and while he be gaze about him , there walk slowly past a flock of the same beautiful bird he have see so many month ago .
Fascinated , he watch them one by one step into the canal , and float quietly upon the water a if they be part of them . '
I will follow them , ' say the duckling to himself ; ` ugly though I be , I would rather be kill by them than suffer all I have suffer from cold and hunger , and from the duck and fowl who should have treat me kindly . '
And fly quickly down to the water , he swim after them as fast a he could .
It do not take him long to reach them , for they have stop to rest in a green pool shade by a tree whose branch sweep the water .
And directly they saw him come some of the young one swim out to meet him with cry of welcome , which again the duckling hardly understood .
He approach them glad , yet tremble , and turn to one of the old bird , who by this time have leave the shade of the tree , he say : ` If I be to die , I would rather you should kill me .
I do n't know why I be ever hatch , for I be too ugly to live . '
And a he speak , he bow his head and look down into the water .
Reflected in the still pool he saw many white shape , with long neck and golden bill , and , without thinking , he look for the dull grey body and the awkward skinny neck .
But no such thing be there .
Instead , he behold beneath him a beautiful white swan !
` The new one be the best of all , ' say the child when they come down to fee the swan with biscuit and cake before go to bed .
` His feather be white and his beak more golden than the rest . '
And when he hear that , the duckling thought that it be worth while have undergone all the persecution and loneliness that he have pass through , a otherwise he would never have know what it be to be really happy .
The Two Caskets -LSB- Hans Andersen . -RSB-
Far , far away , in the midst of a pine forest , there live a woman who have both a daughter and a stepdaughter .
Ever since her own daughter be bear the mother have give her all that she cry for , so she grow up to be a cross and disagreeable a she be ugly .
Her stepsister , on the other hand , have spend her childhood in work hard to keep house for her father , who die soon after his second marriage ; and she be as much beloved by the neighbour for her goodness and industry a she be for her beauty .
As the year go on , the difference between the two girl grow more marked , and the old woman treat her stepdaughter worse than ever , and be always on the watch for some pretext for beat her , or deprive her of her food .
Anything , however foolish , be good enough for this , and one day , when she could think of nothing better , she set both the girl to spin while sit on the low wall of the well .
` And you have good mind what you do , ' say she , ` for the one whose thread break first shall be throw to the bottom . '
But of course she take good care that her own daughter 's flax be fine and strong , while the stepsister have only some coarse stuff , which no one would have think of use .
As might be expect , in a very little while the poor girl 's thread snap , and the old woman , who have be watch from behind a door , seize her stepdaughter by her shoulder , and throw her into the well .
` That be an end of you ! '
she say .
But she be wrong , for it be only the beginning .
Down , down , down go the girl -- it seem a if the well must reach to the very middle of the earth ; but at last her foot touch the ground , and she find herself in a field more beautiful than even the summer pasture of her native mountain .
Trees wave in the soft breeze , and flower of the bright colour dance in the grass .
And though she be quite alone , the girl 's heart dance too , for she felt happy than she have since her father die .
So she walk on through the meadow till she come to an old tumbledown fence -- so old that it be a wonder it manage to stand up at all , and it look a if it depend for support on the old man 's beard that climb all over it .
The girl pause for a moment a she come up , and gaze about for a place where she might safely cross .
But before she could move a voice cry from the fence : ` Do not hurt me , little maiden ; I be so old , so old , I have not much longer to live . '
And the maiden answer : ` No , I will not hurt you ; fear nothing . '
And then see a spot where the clematis grow less thickly than in other place , she jump lightly over .
` May all go well with thee , ' say the fence , a the girl walk on .
She soon leave the meadow and turn into a path which run between two flowery hedge .
Right in front of her stood an oven , and through it open door she could see a pile of white loaf .
` Eat a many loaf a you like , but do me no harm , little maiden , ' cry the oven .
And the maiden tell her to fear nothing , for she never hurt anything , and be very grateful for the oven 's kindness in give her such a beautiful white loaf .
When she have finish it , down to the last crumb , she shut the oven door and say : ` Good-morning . '
` May all go well with thee , ' say the oven , a the girl walk on .
By-and-by she become very thirsty , and see a cow with a milk-pail hanging on her horn , turn towards her .
` Milk me and drink as much a you will , little maiden , ' cry the cow , ` but be sure you spill none on the ground ; and do me no harm , for I have never harm anyone . '
` Nor I , ' answer the girl ; ` fear nothing . '
So she sit down and milk till the pail be nearly full .
Then she drink it all up except a little drop at the bottom .
` Now throw any that be leave over my hoof , and hang the pail on my horn again , ' say the cow .
And the girl do a she be bid , and kiss the cow on her forehead and go her way .
Many hour have now pass since the girl have fall down the well , and the sun be set .
` Where shall I spend the night ? '
think she .
And suddenly she saw before her a gate which she have not notice before , and a very old woman lean against it .
` Good evening , ' say the girl politely ; and the old woman answer : ` Good evening , my child .
Would that everyone be as polite a you .
Are you in search of anything ? ' '
I be in search of a place , ' reply the girl ; and the woman smile and say : ` Then stop a little while and comb my hair , and you shall tell me all the thing you can do . '
` Willingly , mother , ' answer the girl .
And she begin comb out the old woman 's hair , which be long and white .
Half an hour pass in this way , and then the old woman say : ` As you do not think yourself too good to comb me , I will show you where you may take service .
Be prudent and patient and all will go well . '
So the girl thank her , and set out for a farm at a little distance , where she be engage to milk the cow and sift the corn .
As soon a it be light next morning the girl get up and go into the cow-house .
` I 'm sure you must be hungry , ' say she , pat each in turn .
And then she fetch hay from the barn , and while they be eat it , she sweep out the cow-house , and strew clean straw upon the floor .
The cow be so pleased with the care she take of them that they stand quite still while she milk them , and do not play any of the trick on her that they have play on other dairymaid who be rough and rude .
And when she have do , and be go to get up from her stool , she find sit round her a whole circle of cat , black and white , tabby and tortoise-shell , who all cry with one voice : ` We be very thirsty , please give u some milk ! '
` My poor little pussy , ' say she , ` of course you shall have some . '
And she go into the dairy , follow by all the cat , and give each one a little red saucerful .
But before they drink they all rub themselves against her knee and purr by way of thanks .
The next thing the girl have to do be to go to the storehouse , and to sift the corn through a sieve .
While she be busy rub the corn she hear a whirr of wing , and a flock of sparrow fly in at the window .
` We be hungry ; give u some corn !
give u some corn ! '
cry they ; and the girl answer : ` You poor little bird , of course you shall have some ! '
and scatter a fine handful over the floor .
When they have finish they fly on her shoulder and flap their wing by way of thanks .
Time go by , and no cow in the whole country-side be so fat and well tend a hers , and no dairy have so much milk to show .
The farmer 's wife be so well satisfied that she give her high wage , and treat her like her own daughter .
At length , one day , the girl be bid by her mistress to come into the kitchen , and when there , the old woman say to her : ' I know you can tend cow and keep a diary ; now let me see what you can do besides .
Take this sieve to the well , and fill it with water , and bring it home to me without spill one drop by the way . '
The girl 's heart sank at this order ; for how be it possible for her to do her mistress 's bidding ?
However , she be silent , and take the sieve go down to the well with it .
Stopping over the side , she fill it to the brim , but as soon a she lift it the water all run out of the hole .
Again and again she try , but not a drop would remain in the sieve , and she be just turn away in despair when a flock of sparrow fly down from the sky .
` Ashes !
ash ! '
they twitter ; and the girl look at them and say : ` Well , I ca n't be in a bad plight than I be already , so I will take your advice . '
And she run back to the kitchen and fill her sieve with ash .
Then once more she dip the sieve into the well , and , behold , this time not a drop of water disappear !
` Here be the sieve , mistress , ' cry the girl , go to the room where the old woman be sit .
` You be clever than I expect , ' answer she ; ` or else someone help you who be skilled in magic . '
But the girl keep silence , and the old woman ask her no more question .
Many day pass during which the girl go about her work as usual , but at length one day the old woman call her and say : ' I have something more for you to do .
There be here two yarn , the one white , the other black .
What you must do be to wash them in the river till the black one become white and the white black . '
And the girl take them to the river and wash hard for several hour , but wash a she would they never change one whit .
` This be bad than the sieve , ' think she , and be about to give up in despair when there come a rush of wing through the air , and on every twig of the birch tree which grow by the bank be perch a sparrow .
` The black to the east , the white to the west ! '
they sing , all at once ; and the girl dry her tear and felt brave again .
Picking up the black yarn , she stand face the east and dip it in the river , and in an instant it grow white a snow , then turn to the west , she hold the white yarn in the water , and it become as black a a crow 's wing .
She look back at the sparrow and smile and nod to them , and flap their wing in reply they fly swiftly away .
At the sight of the yarn the old woman be strike dumb ; but when at length she find her voice she ask the girl what magician have help her to do what no one have do before .
But she get no answer , for the maiden be afraid of bring trouble on her little friend .
For many week the mistress shut herself up in her room , and the girl go about her work as usual .
She hop that there be an end to the difficult task which have be set her ; but in this she be mistake , for one day the old woman appear suddenly in the kitchen , and say to her : ` There be one more trial to which I must put you , and if you do not fail in that you will be leave in peace for evermore .
Here be the yarn which you wash .
Take them and weave them into a web that be as smooth a a king 's robe , and see that it be spin by the time that the sun set . '
` This be the easy thing I have be set to do , ' think the girl , who be a good spinner .
But when she begin she find that the skein tangle and break every moment .
` Oh , I can never do it ! '
she cry at last , and lean her head against the loom and wept ; but at that instant the door open , and there enter , one behind another , a procession of cat .
` What be the matter , fair maiden ? '
ask they .
And the girl answer : ` My mistress have give me this yarn to weave into a piece of cloth , which must be finish by sunset , and I have not even begin yet , for the yarn break whenever I touch it . '
` If that be all , dry your eye , ' say the cat ; ` we will manage it for you . '
And they jump on the loom , and weave so fast and so skilfully that in a very short time the cloth be ready and be as fine a any king ever wore .
The girl be so delighted at the sight of it that she give each cat a kiss on his forehead a they leave the room behind one the other a they have come .
` Who have teach you this wisdom ? '
ask the old woman , after she have pass her hand twice or thrice over the cloth and could find no roughness anywhere .
But the girl only smile and do not answer .
She have learn early the value of silence .
After a few week the old woman send for her maid and tell her that a her year of service be now up , she be free to return home , but that , for her part , the girl have serve her so well that she hop she might stay with her .
But at these word the maid shake her head , and answer gently : ' I have be happy here , Madam , and I thank you for your goodness to me ; but I have leave behind me a stepsister and a stepmother , and I be fain to be with them once more . '
The old woman look at her for a moment , and then she say : ` Well , that must be a you like ; but a you have work faithfully for me I will give you a reward .
Go now into the loft above the store house and there you will find many casket .
Choose the one which please you best , but be careful not to open it till you have set it in the place where you wish it to remain . '
The girl leave the room to go to the loft , and as soon a she get outside , she find all the cat wait for her .
Walking in procession , a be their custom , they follow her into the loft , which be fill with casket big and little , plain and splendid .
She lift up one and look at it , and then put it down to examine another yet more beautiful .
Which should she choose , the yellow or the blue , the red or the green , the gold or the silver ?
She hesitate long , and go first to one and then to another , when she hear the cat ' voice call : ` Take the black !
take the black ! '
The word make her look round -- she have see no black casket , but a the cat continue their cry she peer into several corner that have remain unnoticed , and at length discover a little black box , so small and so black , that it might easily have be pass over .
` This be the casket that please me best , mistress , ' say the girl , carry it into the house .
And the old woman smile and nod , and bid her go her way .
So the girl set forth , after bid farewell to the cow and the cat and the sparrow , who all weep a they say good-bye .
She walk on and on and on , till she reach the flowery meadow , and there , suddenly , something happen , she never know what , but she be sit on the wall of the well in her stepmother 's yard .
Then she get up and enter the house .
The woman and her daughter star a if they have be turn into stone ; but at length the stepmother gasp out : ` So you be alive after all !
Well , luck be ever against me !
And where have you be this year past ? '
Then the girl tell how she have take service in the under-world , and , beside her wage , have bring home with her a little casket , which she would like to set up in her room .
` Give me the money , and take the ugly little box off to the outhouse , ' cry the woman , beside herself with rage , and the girl , quite frighten at her violence , hasten away , with her precious box clasp to her bosom .
The outhouse be in a very dirty state , a no one have be near it since the girl have fall down the well ; but she scrub and sweep till everything be clean again , and then she place the little casket on a small shelf in the corner .
` Now I may open it , ' she say to herself ; and unlock it with the key which hang to it handle , she raise the lid , but start back a she do so , almost blind by the light that burst upon her .
No one would ever have guess that that little black box could have hold such a quantity of beautiful thing !
Rings , crown , girdle , necklace -- all make of wonderful stone ; and they shine with such brilliance that not only the stepmother and her daughter but all the people round come run to see if the house be on fire .
Of course the woman felt quite ill with greed and envy , and she would have certainly take all the jewel for herself have she not fear the wrath of the neighbour , who love her stepdaughter as much a they hat her .
But if she could not steal the casket and it content for herself , at least she could get another like it , and perhaps a still rich one .
So she bid her own daughter sit on the edge of the well , and throw her into the water , exactly a she have do to the other girl ; and , exactly a before , the flowery meadow lay at the bottom .
Every inch of the way she tread the path which her stepsister have tread , and saw the thing which she have see ; but there the likeness end .
When the fence pray her to do it no harm , she laugh rudely , and tear up some of the stake so that she might get over the more easily ; when the oven offer her bread , she scatter the loaf onto the ground and stamp on them ; and after she have milk the cow , and drink as much a she want , she throw the rest on the grass , and kick the pail to bit , and never hear them say , a they look after her : ` You shall not have do this to me for nothing ! '
Towards even she reach the spot where the old woman be lean against the gate-post , but she pass her by without a word .
` Have you no manner in your country ? '
ask the crone . '
I ca n't stop and talk ; I be in a hurry , ' answer the girl .
` It be get late , and I have to find a place . '
` Stop and comb my hair for a little , ' say the old woman , ` and I will help you to get a place . '
` Comb your hair , indeed !
I have something well to do than that ! '
And slam the gate in the crone 's face she go her way .
And she never hear the word that follow her : ` You shall not have do this to me for nothing ! '
By-and-by the girl arrive at the farm , and she be engage to look after the cow and sift the corn a her stepsister have be .
But it be only when someone be watch her that she do her work ; at other time the cow-house be dirty , and the cow ill-fed and beaten , so that they kick over the pail , and try to butt her ; and everyone say they have never see such thin cow or such poor milk .
As for the cat , she chase them away , and ill-treated them , so that they have not even the spirit to chase the rat and mouse , which nowadays ran about everywhere .
And when the sparrow come to beg for some corn , they fare no good than the cow and the cat , for the girl throw her shoe at them , till they fly in a fright to the wood , and take shelter amongst the tree .
Months pass in this manner , when , one day , the mistress call the girl to her .
` All that I have give you to do you have do ill , ' say she , ` yet will I give you another chance .
For though you can not tend cow , or divide the grain from the chaff , there may be other thing that you can do good .
Therefore take this sieve to the well , and fill it with water , and see that you bring it back without spill a drop . '
The girl take the sieve and carry it to the well a her sister have do ; but no little bird come to help her , and after dip it in the well two or three time she bring it back empty . '
I think as much , ' say the old woman angrily ; ` she that be useless in one thing be useless in another . '
Perhaps the mistress may have think that the girl have learn a lesson , but , if she do , she be quite mistaken , a the work be no good do than before .
By-and-by she send for her again , and give her maid the black and white yarn to wash in the river ; but there be no one to tell her the secret by which the black would turn white , and the white black ; so she bring them back a they be .
This time the old woman only look at her grimly but the girl be too well pleased with herself to care what anyone think about her .
After some week her third trial come , and the yarn be give her to spin , a it have be give to her stepsister before her .
But no procession of cat enter the room to weave a web of fine cloth , and at sunset she only bring back to her mistress an armful of dirty , tangle wool .
` There seem nothing in the world you can do , ' say the old woman , and leave her to herself .
Soon after this the year be up , and the girl go to her mistress to tell her that she wish to go home .
` Little desire have I to keep you , ' answer the old woman , ` for no one thing have you do a you ought .
Still , I will give you some payment , therefore go up into the loft , and choose for yourself one of the casket that lie there .
But see that you do not open it till you place it where you wish it to stay . '
This be what the girl have be hop for , and so rejoice be she , that , without even stop to thank the old woman , she run as fast a she could to the loft .
There be the casket , blue and red , green and yellow , silver and gold ; and there in the corner stand a little black casket just like the one her stepsister have bring home .
` If there be so many jewel in that little black thing , this big red one will hold twice the number , ' she say to herself ; and snatch it up she set off on her road home without even go to bid farewell to her mistress .
` See , mother , see what I have bring ! '
cry she , a she enter the cottage hold the casket in both hand .
` Ah !
you have get something very different from that little black box , ' answer the old woman with delight .
But the girl be so busy find a place for it to stand that she take little notice of her mother .
` It will look best here -- no , here , ' she say , set it first on one piece of furniture and then on another .
` No , after all it be to fine to live in a kitchen , let u place it in the guest chamber . '
So mother and daughter carry it proudly upstairs and put it on a shelf over the fireplace ; then , untie the key from the handle , they open the box .
As before , a bright light leapt out directly the lid be raise , but it do not spring from the lustre of jewel , but from hot flame , which dart along the wall and burn up the cottage and all that be in it and the mother and daughter as well .
As they have do when the stepdaughter come home , the neighbour all hurry to see what be the matter ; but they be too late .
Only the hen-house be leave standing ; and , in spite of her rich , there the stepdaughter live happily to the end of her day .
The Goldsmith 's Fortune -LSB- From Thorpe 's Yule-Tide Stories . -RSB-
Once upon a time there be a goldsmith who live in a certain village where the people be a bad and greedy , and covetous , a they could possibly be ; however , in spite of his surroundings , he be fat and prosperous .
He have only one friend whom he like , and that be a cowherd , who look after cattle for one of the farmer in the village .
Every even the goldsmith would walk across to the cowherd 's house and say : ` Come , let 's go out for a walk ! '
Now the cowherd do n't like walk in the evening , because , he say , he have be out graze the cattle all day , and be glad to sit down when night come ; but the goldsmith always worry him so that the poor man have to go against his will .
This at last so annoy him that he try to think how he could pick a quarrel with the goldsmith , so that he should not beg him to walk with him any more .
He ask another cowherd for advice , and he say the best thing he could do be to go across and kill the goldsmith 's wife , for then the goldsmith would be sure to regard him a an enemy ; so , be a foolish person , and there be no law in that country by which a man would be certainly punish for such a crime , the cowherd one evening take a big stick and go across to the goldsmith 's house when only Mrs. Goldsmith be at home , and bang her on the head so hard that she die then and there .
When the goldsmith come back and find his wife dead he say nothing , but just take her outside into the dark lane and prop her up against the wall of his house , and then go into the courtyard and wait .
Presently a rich stranger come along the lane , and see someone there , a he suppose , he say : ` Good-evening , friend !
a fine night to-night ! '
But the goldsmith 's wife say nothing .
The man then repeat his word louder ; but still there be no reply .
A third time he shout : ` Good-evening , friend !
be you deaf ? '
but the figure never reply .
Then the stranger , be angry at what he think very rude behaviour , pick up a big stone and throw it at Mrs. Goldsmith , cry : ` Let that teach you manner ! '
Instantly poor Mrs. Goldsmith tumble over ; and the stranger , horrify at see what he have do , be immediately seize by the goldsmith , who run out screaming : ` Wretch !
you have kill my wife !
Oh , miserable one ; we will have justice do to thee ! '
With many protestation and reproach they wrangle together , the stranger entreat the goldsmith to say nothing and he would pay him handsomely to atone for the sad accident .
At last the goldsmith quiet down , and agree to accept one thousand gold piece from the stranger , who immediately help him to bury his poor wife , and then rush off to the guest house , pack up his thing and be off by daylight , lest the goldsmith should repent and accuse him a the murderer of his wife .
Now it very soon appear that the goldsmith have a lot of extra money , so that people begin to ask question , and finally demand of him the reason for his sudden wealth .
` Oh , ' say he , ` my wife die , and I sell her . '
` You sell your dead wife ? '
cry the people .
` Yes , ' say the goldsmith .
` For how much ? ' '
A thousand gold piece , ' reply the goldsmith .
Instantly the villager go away and each caught hold of his own wife and throttle her , and the next day they all go off to sell their dead wife .
Many a weary mile do they tramp , but get nothing but hard word or laughter , or direction to the near cemetery , from people to whom they offer dead wife for sale .
At last they perceive that they have be cheat somehow by that goldsmith .
So off they rush home , seize the unhappy man , and , without listen to his cry and entreaty , hurry him down to the river bank and flung him -- plop !
-- into the deep , weedy , and nasty place they could find .
` That will teach him to play trick on u , ' say they .
` For a he ca n't swim he 'll drown , and we sha 'n ' t have any more trouble with him ! '
Now the goldsmith really could not swim , and as soon a he be throw into the deep river he sink below the surface ; so his enemy go away believe that they have see the last of him .
But , in reality , he be carry down , half drown , below the next bend in the river , where he fortunately come across a ` snag ' float in the water -LRB- a snag be , you know , a part of a tree or bush which float very nearly under the surface of the water -RRB- ; and he hold on to this snag , and by great good luck eventually come ashore some two or three mile down the river .
At the place where he land he come across a fine fat cow buffalo , and immediately he jump on her back and ride home .
When the village people saw him , they run out in surprise , and say : ` Where on earth do you come from , and where do you get that buffalo ? '
` Ah ! '
say the goldsmith , ` you little know what delightful adventure I have have !
Why , down in that place in the river where you throw me in I find meadow , and tree , and fine pasture , and buffalo , and all kind of cattle .
In fact , I could hardly tear myself away ; but I think that I must really let you all know about it . '
` Oh , oh ! '
think the greedy village people ; ` if there be buffalo to be have for the taking we 'll go after some too . '

And whenever any one of them wave his hand about and struggle the goldsmith would cry out : ` Look !
he 's beckon the rest of you to come ; he 's get a fine buffalo ! '
And others who be doubtful would jump in , until not one be leave .
Then the cunning goldsmith go back and take all the village for himself , and become very rich indeed .
But do you think he be happy ?
Not a bit .
Lies never make a man happy yet .
Truly , he get the good of a set of wicked and greedy people , but only by be wicked and greedy himself ; and , a it turn out , when he get so rich he get very fat ; and at last be so fat that he could n't move , and one day he get the apoplexy and die , and no one in the world care the least bit .
The Enchanted Wreath -LSB- Told by a Pathan to Major Campbell . -RSB-
Once upon a time there live near a forest a man and his wife and two girl ; one girl be the daughter of the man , and the other the daughter of his wife ; and the man 's daughter be good and beautiful , but the woman 's daughter be cross and ugly .
However , her mother do not know that , but think her the most bewitching maiden that ever be see .
One day the man call to his daughter and bid her come with him into the forest to cut wood .
They work hard all day , but in spite of the chop they be very cold , for it rain heavily , and when they return home , they be wet through .
Then , to his vexation , the man find that he have leave his axe behind him , and he know that if it lay all night in the mud it would become rusty and useless .
So he say to his wife : ' I have drop my axe in the forest , bid your daughter go and fetch it , for mine have work hard all day and be both wet and weary . '
But the wife answer : ` If your daughter be wet already , it be all the more reason that she should go and get the axe .
Besides , she be a great strong girl , and a little rain will not hurt her , while my daughter would be sure to catch a bad cold . '
By long experience the man know there be no good say any more , and with a sigh he tell the poor girl she must return to the forest for the axe .

The Foolish Weaver -LSB- Adapted from Thorpe 's Yule-Tide Stories . -RSB-
Once a weaver , who be in want of work , take service with a certain farmer a a shepherd .
The farmer , know that the man be very slow-witted , give him most careful instruction a to everything that he be to do .
Finally he say : ` If a wolf or any wild animal attempt to hurt the flock you should pick up a big stone like this ' -LRB- suit the action to the word -RRB- ` and throw a few such at him , and he will be afraid and go away . '
The weaver say that he understand , and start with the flock to the hillside where they graze all day .
By chance in the afternoon a leopard appear , and the weaver instantly run home as fast a he could to get the stone which the farmer have show him , to throw at the creature .
When he come back all the flock be scatter or kill , and when the farmer hear the tale he beat him soundly .
` Were there no stone on the hillside that you should run back to get them , you senseless one ? '
he cry ; ` you be not fit to herd sheep .
To-day you shall stay at home and mind my old mother who be sick , perhaps you will be able to drive fly off her face , if you ca n't drive beast away from sheep ! '
So , the next day , the weaver be leave at home to take care of the farmer 's old sick mother .
Now a she lay outside on a bed , it turn out that the fly become very troublesome , and the weaver look round for something to drive them away with ; and a he have be tell to pick up the near stone to drive the beast away from the flock , he think he would this time show how cleverly he could obey order .
Accordingly he seize the near stone , which be a big , heavy one , and dash it at the fly ; but , unhappily , he slew the poor old woman also ; and then , be afraid of the wrath of the farmer , he flee and be not see again in that neighbourhood .
All that day and all the next night he walk , and at length he come to a village where a great many weaver live together .
` You be welcome , ' say they .
` Eat and sleep , for to-morrow six of u start in search of fresh wool to weave , and we pray you to give u your company . '
` Willingly , ' answer the weaver .
So the next morning the seven weaver set out to go to the village where they could buy what they want .
On the way they have to cross a ravine which lately have be full of water , but now be quite dry .
The weaver , however , be accustom to swim over this ravine ; therefore , regardless of the fact that this time it be dry , they strip , and , tie their clothes on their head , they proceed to swim across the dry sand and rock that form the bed of the ravine .
Thus they get to the other side without further damage than bruise knee and elbow , and as soon a they be over , one of them begin to count the party to make sure that all be safe there .
He count all except himself , and then cry out that somebody be miss !
This set each of them count ; but each make the same mistake of count all except himself , so that they become certain that one of their party be miss !
They run up and down the bank of the ravine wring their hand in great distress and look for sign of their lose comrade .
There a farmer find them and ask what be the matter .
` Alas ! '
say one , ` seven of u start from the other bank and one must have be drown on the crossing , a we can only find six remain ! '
The farmer eye them a minute , and then , pick up his stick , he deal each a sounding blow , counting , a he do so , ` One !
two !
three ! '
and so on up to the seven .
When the weaver find that there be seven of them they be overcome with gratitude to one whom they take for a magician a he could thus make seven out of an obvious six .
The Clever Cat -LSB- From the Pushto . -RSB-
Once upon a time there live an old man who dwell with his son in a small hut on the edge of the plain .
He be very old , and have work very hard , and when at last he be strike down by illness he felt that he should never rise from his bed again .
So , one day , he bid his wife summon their son , when he come back from his journey to the near town , where he have be to buy bread .
` Come hither , my son , ' say he ; ' I know myself well to be die , and I have nothing to leave you but my falcon , my cat and my greyhound ; but if you make good use of them you will never lack food .
Be good to your mother , a you have be to me .
And now farewell ! '
Then he turn his face to the wall and die .
There be great mourning in the hut for many day , but at length the son rise up , and call to his greyhound , his cat and his falcon , he leave the house say that he would bring back something for dinner .
Wandering over the plain , he notice a troop of gazelle , and point to his greyhound to give chase .
The dog soon bring down a fine fat beast , and sling it over his shoulder , the young man turn homewards .
On the way , however , he pass a pond , and a he approach a cloud of bird fly into the air .

The young man pick it up , and put it in his pouch and then go towards home again .
Near the hut be a small barn in which he keep the produce of the little patch of corn , which grow close to the garden .
Here a rat run out almost under his foot , follow by another and another ; but quick a thought the cat be upon them and not one escape her .
When all the rat be kill , the young man leave the barn .
He take the path lead to the door of the hut , but stop on feel a hand lay on his shoulder .
` Young man , ' say the ogre -LRB- for such be the stranger -RRB- , ` you have be a good son , and you deserve the piece of luck which have befall you this day .
Come with me to that shin lake yonder , and fear nothing . '
Wondering a little at what might be go to happen to him , the youth do a the ogre bid him , and when they reach the shore of the lake , the ogre turn and say to him : ` Step into the water and shut your eye !
You will find yourself sink slowly to the bottom ; but take courage , all will go well .
Only bring up as much silver a you can carry , and we will divide it between u . '
So the young man step bravely into the lake , and felt himself sinking , sinking , till he reach firm ground at last .
In front of him lay four heap of silver , and in the midst of them a curious white shin stone , mark over with strange character , such a he have never see before .
He pick it up in order to examine it more closely , and a he hold it the stone speak .
` As long a you hold me , all your wish will come true , ' it say .
` But hide me in your turban , and then call to the ogre that you be ready to come up . '
In a few minute the young man stand again by the shore of the lake .
` Well , where be the silver ? '
ask the ogre , who be await him .
` Ah , my father , how can I tell you !

And he shut them ; and when he open them again the camel that he have wish for be stand before him , while the festal robe of a desert prince hung from his shoulder .

At these word the mother think her son have certainly go mad , and star blankly at him .

So she go to the palace , where she find the king sit in the Hall of Justice listen to the petition of his people .
The woman wait until all have be hear and the hall be empty , and then go up and kneel before the throne .
` My son have send me to ask for the hand of the princess , ' say she .
The king look at her and think that she be mad ; but , instead of order his guard to turn her out , he answer gravely : ` Before he can marry the princess he must build me a palace of ice , which can be warm with fire , and wherein the rare singing-birds can live ! '

persist he .
